## **3. Energy Auditing**

> Process of evaluating how energy is used and finding opportunities for savings.

**Types:**
| Type | Depth | Output |
|-------|----------|-----------|
| **Preliminary Audit** | Quick survey | Basic data |
| **Detailed Audit** | Comprehensive analysis | Cost-benefit report |

**Steps:**
1. Data collection (bills, equipment rating)  
2. Field measurements  
3. Energy flow diagram  
4. Identify losses  
5. Recommendations  

---

## **4. Energy Conservation Techniques**

| System | Techniques |
|-----------|----------------|
| **Lighting** | LED, daylight sensors, motion control |
| **Motors** | Variable Frequency Drive (VFD), high-efficiency motors |
| **Pumps/Fans** | Impeller trimming, flow optimization |
| **Building** | Thermal insulation, reflective roofs |
| **HVAC** | Smart thermostats, demand-controlled ventilation |

---

## **5. HVAC Systems (Heating, Ventilation, and Air Conditioning)**

> HVAC is a major component of building energy use (~40%).

**Key Components:**
- **Chillers / Boilers:** Heat generation  
- **Air Handling Unit (AHU):** Air distribution  
- **Cooling Tower:** Heat rejection  
- **Ducts & Vents:** Air flow control  

**Efficiency Measures:**
- Use **Variable Speed Drives (VSDs)**  
- Install **Heat Recovery Systems**  
- Maintain **Coefficient of Performance (COP)** > 4.0  
- Implement **Zoning** for air distribution  

---

## **6. Building Envelope and Thermal Performance**

> The **Building Envelope** controls heat transfer between interior and exterior.

**Elements:**
- Roof  
- Walls  
- Windows and doors  
- Insulation materials  

**Parameters:**
- **U-Value:** Heat transmission rate  
- **R-Value:** Thermal resistance  

**Improvement Methods:**
- Double-glazed windows  
- Reflective roof coatings  
- Green roofs & vertical gardens  

---

## **7. Green Building Concept**

> A Green Building is designed to reduce energy consumption, water use, and environmental impact while improving occupant comfort.

**Key Principles:**
- Sustainable site planning  
- Energy efficiency  
- Water efficiency  
- Indoor environmental quality  
- Use of renewable resources  

---

## **8. LEED and GRIHA Rating Systems**

**LEED (Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design):**
- International green certification system  
- Developed by USGBC  
- Points-based rating (Certified â†’ Silver â†’ Gold â†’ Platinum)

**GRIHA (Green Rating for Integrated Habitat Assessment):**
- Indiaâ€™s national green building rating system  
- Evaluates building lifecycle energy use  

**Criteria:**
- Site selection  
- Energy conservation  
- Renewable integration  
- Waste management  

---

## **9. Passive Design Techniques**

> Passive design minimizes energy use by using natural elements (sun, wind, shade).

**Examples:**
- Orientation (East-West axis for daylighting)  
- Cross ventilation  
- Thermal mass (walls that store heat)  
- Shading devices (louvers, overhangs)  

**Benefit:** 30â€“50% cooling energy reduction  

---

## **10. Renewable Energy in Buildings**

| Source | Application |
|-----------|----------------|
| **Solar PV** | Rooftop power generation |
| **Solar Thermal** | Hot water |
| **Wind Turbine** | High-rise integration |
| **Biogas** | Kitchen fuel |
| **Geothermal** | Ground-source HVAC |

**Example:**  
Net Zero Energy Buildings (NZEBs) â€“ generate as much energy as they consume.

---

## **11. Water and Waste Management**

- **Rainwater Harvesting**  
- **Greywater Recycling**  
- **Low-flow Fixtures**  
- **Organic Waste Composting**  
- **Sewage Treatment Plants (STPs)**  

**Objective:** Achieve *Water Neutrality* and *Zero Waste* operation.

---

## **12. Smart Building Automation Systems (BAS)**

> Uses IoT and AI to monitor and control lighting, HVAC, and security automatically.

**Functions:**
- Occupancy sensors  
- Temperature optimization  
- Energy data analytics  
- Fault detection and diagnostics  

**Standards:** BACnet, KNX, Modbus  

**Benefits:**  
- Up to 25% energy savings  
- Improved comfort & reliability  

---

## **13. Smart Cities and Urban Energy Planning**

> Smart Cities integrate technology and sustainability for efficient living.

**Components:**
- Smart grids

## **15. Carbon Neutral and Net Zero Concepts**

> **Carbon Neutrality** means total COâ‚‚ emitted = COâ‚‚ absorbed or offset.

**Strategies:**
- Use of renewable power  
- Energy-efficient design  
- Carbon capture technologies  
- Offsetting (tree plantation, carbon credits)  

**Net Zero Building:**
- On-site renewable generation = Total annual energy consumption  

---

- Smart Meters

## **Overview**

## **1. Meaning of Decarbonization**

> **Decarbonization** á€†á€­á€¯á€á€¬á€€ á€¡á€“á€­á€€á€¡á€¬á€¸á€–á€¼á€„á€·á€º  
> á€“á€¬á€á€ºá€„á€½á€±á€·áŠ á€†á€®áŠ á€™á€®á€¸á€á€½á€±á€¸á€œá€­á€¯ á€…á€½á€™á€ºá€¸á€¡á€„á€ºá€›á€„á€ºá€¸á€™á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€½á€±á€€  
> á€‘á€¯á€á€ºá€œá€½á€¾á€„á€·á€ºá€á€²á€· COâ‚‚ á€€á€­á€¯ á€œá€»á€¾á€±á€¬á€·á€á€»á€–á€­á€¯á€· á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

## **2. The Net Zero 2050 Vision**

|-------------|----------------|
| **Electrification** | Replace fossil-based processes with electricity |

## **5. Sector-Wise Decarbonization**

### **a) Power Sector**
- Transition from Coal â†’ Gas â†’ Renewables  
- Integration of **Solar, Wind, Hydro, Geothermal**  
- Deployment of **HVDC Transmission** for long-distance clean power transfer  

### **b) Transport Sector**
- Electrification (EVs, hydrogen fuel cells)  
- Railway electrification  
- Sustainable aviation fuel (SAF) development  

### **c) Industrial Sector**
- Electrification of process heating  
- Green hydrogen for steel, cement, ammonia  
- Carbon capture at factories  

### **d) Buildings**
- Net Zero Energy Buildings (NZEBs)  
- Heat pumps replacing gas boilers  
- Smart metering and automation

## **6. Role of Hydrogen in Decarbonization**

> Hydrogen acts as the â€œmissing linkâ€ for deep decarbonization.

| Type | Source | Emission Level |
|-----------|-------------|----------------|
| **Grey Hydrogen** | Natural gas | High |
| **Blue Hydrogen** | Gas + Carbon Capture | Medium |
| **Green Hydrogen** | Renewable electrolysis | Zero |

**Applications:**
- Fuel cells for transport  
- Industrial feedstock  
- Energy storage for grid stability  

---

## **7. Carbon Capture, Utilization, and Storage (CCUS)**

> Capturing COâ‚‚ from emission sources and reusing or storing it underground.

**Methods:**
- Post-combustion (flue gas treatment)  
- Pre-combustion (gasification)  
- Direct Air Capture (DAC)  

**Storage:**  
Depleted oil fields, saline aquifers  

**Utilization:**  
E-fuels, synthetic materials

**Youngâ€™s Modulus** á€á€Šá€º material á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€›á€²á€· á€á€Šá€ºá€„á€¼á€­á€™á€ºá€™á€¾á€¯á€€á€­á€¯ á€á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€¸á€á€¬á€á€²á€· á€¡á€á€»á€€á€ºá€¡á€œá€€á€ºá€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

> (Example: Steel > Aluminium > Rubber)

## **Poissonâ€™s Ratio (Î¼)**

Material á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€€á€­á€¯ á€†á€½á€²á€á€»á€­á€”á€ºá€™á€¾á€¬ á€¡á€›á€¾á€Šá€ºá€á€­á€¯á€¸á€á€œá€­á€¯ á€¡á€€á€»á€šá€ºá€œá€»á€¾á€±á€¬á€·á€á€á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

\[

**Typical Values**

## **Introduction**

Beam á€†á€­á€¯á€á€¬á€Ÿá€¬ Load á€€á€­á€¯á€á€Šá€ºá€‘á€±á€¬á€„á€ºá€‘á€¬á€¸á€á€²á€· Supports á€á€½á€±á€€á€¼á€¬á€¸á€™á€¾á€¬ á€á€šá€ºá€šá€°á€•á€±á€¸á€á€²á€·  
Structural Member á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹  

Beam á€¡á€•á€±á€«á€ºá€™á€¾á€¬ Load á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€á€€á€ºá€œá€¬á€á€²á€·á€¡á€á€«  
Internal forces á€”á€¾á€…á€ºá€™á€»á€­á€¯á€¸á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€²á€· **Shear Force (S.F)** á€”á€²á€· **Bending Moment (B.M)** á€•á€±á€«á€ºá€œá€¬á€á€šá€ºá‹  
á€’á€®á€¡á€„á€ºá€¡á€¬á€¸á€”á€¾á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€€á€­á€¯ á€á€­á€™á€¾ Beam á€¡á€€á€¼á€¶á€‰á€¬á€á€ºá€¡á€á€½á€€á€º á€œá€¯á€¶á€¸á€á€¡á€á€¼á€±á€á€¶á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

---

## **Definition of Shear Force (S.F.)**

**Shear Force (F)** á€†á€­á€¯á€á€¬á€Ÿá€¬  
Beam á€á€…á€ºá€•á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€¸á€€á€­á€¯ á€–á€¼á€á€ºá€œá€­á€¯á€€á€ºá€á€²á€·á€¡á€á€«,  
á€–á€¼á€á€ºá€‘á€¬á€¸á€á€²á€· Section á€”á€¾á€…á€ºá€–á€€á€ºá€™á€¾á€¬ á€á€…á€ºá€–á€€á€ºá€á€…á€ºá€œá€™á€ºá€¸á€á€½á€¬á€¸á€¡á€„á€ºá€¡á€¬á€¸á€á€½á€±á€”á€²á€·  
Balance á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€¡á€±á€¬á€„á€º á€‘á€­á€”á€ºá€¸á€•á€±á€¸á€á€²á€· Internal Force á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

\[
F = \text{Sum of Vertical Forces (Left or Right of Section)}
\]

> Positive S.F â†’ Left side upward reaction  
> Negative S.F â†’ Left side downward reaction

---

## **Definition of Bending Moment (B.M.)**

**Bending Moment (M)** á€†á€­á€¯á€á€¬á€Ÿá€¬  
Beam á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€™á€¾á€¬ á€œá€¾á€Šá€·á€ºá€á€½á€„á€·á€ºá€•á€±á€¸á€á€²á€· á€¡á€„á€ºá€¡á€¬á€¸á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹  
Load á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€á€€á€ºá€œá€¬á€á€²á€·á€¡á€á€« Beam á€€á€­á€¯ á€á€¯á€¶á€¸á€á€½á€¬á€¸á€¡á€±á€¬á€„á€º á€œá€¾á€Šá€·á€ºá€•á€±á€¸á€á€á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

\[
M = \text{Sum of Moments about the Section}
\]
\[
M = F Ã— x
\]

| Symbol | Meaning | Unit |
|---------|----------|------|
| M | Bending Moment | NÂ·m |
| F | Shear Force | N |
| x | Distance from section | m |

> Positive B.M â†’ Sagging (Beam curves downward like a smile ğŸ˜Š)  
> Negative B.M â†’ Hogging (Beam curves upward like a frown â˜¹ï¸)

---

## **Types of Beams**

| Type | Description | Example |
|-------|--------------|----------|
| **Simply Supported Beam** | Supported at both ends; can freely rotate. | Bridges, floors |
| **Cantilever Beam** | Fixed at one end, free at the other. | Balcony, crane arm |
| **Overhanging Beam** | Extends beyond one or both supports. | Roof extension |
| **Continuous Beam** | Supported on more than two supports. | Long span bridges |
| **Fixed Beam** | Both ends rigidly fixed. | Frames, walls |

---

## **Types of Loads**

| Type | Symbol | Description |
|-------|----------|--------------|
| **Point Load (W)** | N | Single concentrated load at one point. |
| **Uniformly Distributed Load (UDL)** | w (N/m) | Load evenly spread along length. |
| **Uniformly Varying Load (UVL)** | â€“ | Load varies linearly (triangular pattern). |

---

## **Relationship Between Load, Shear Force, and Bending Moment**

\[
\frac{dF}{dx} = -w
\]
\[
\frac{dM}{dx} = F
\]

> Meaning â€“  
> The slope of the **S.F. diagram** equals the negative of the load intensity,  
> and the slope of the **B.M. diagram** equals the shear force.

---

## **Sign Conventions**

| Condition | Shear Force | Bending Moment |
|------------|--------------|----------------|
| Left portion tending to move **upward** | Positive | Sagging (Positive) |
| Left portion tending to move **downward** | Negative | Hogging (Negative) |

---

## **Shear Force and Bending Moment Diagrams (S.F.D & B.M.D)**

**Purpose:**  
To visualize how internal forces vary along the beam.

**Steps to Draw:**
1. Determine all reactions at supports (using equilibrium).  
2. Move from left to right, summing up forces and moments.  
3. Plot Shear Force and Bending Moment at key points.  
4. Join the points according to the loading type.

---

### **Typical Cases**

#### **Case 1 â€“ Simply Supported Beam with Central Point Load (W)**
\[
R_A = R_B = \frac{W}{2}
\]
\[
M_{max} = \frac{WL}{4}
\]

**S.F.D:** Step function â€“ Positive then Negative.  
**B.M.D:** Parabolic (max at center).

---

#### **Case 2 â€“ Simply Supported Beam with UDL (w per meter)**
\[
R_A = R_B = \frac{wL}{2}
\]
\[
M_{max} = \frac{wL^2}{8}
\]

**S.F.D:** Linear variation.  
**B.M.D:** Parabolic shape.

---

#### **Case 3 â€“ Cantilever Beam with End Load (W)**
\[
M_{max} = WL
\]
\[
F = W
\]

**S.F.D:** Constant.  
**B.M.D:** Linear variation (Max at fixed end).

---

#### **Case 4 â€“ Cantilever Beam with UDL**
\[
F = wL
\]
\[
M_{max} = \frac{wL^2}{2}
\]

---

## **Point of Contraflexure**

Beam á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€™á€¾á€¬ Bending Moment á€á€„á€ºá€¹á€á€»á€¬á€¡á€”á€±á€”á€²á€·  
Positive á€™á€¾ Negative á€á€­á€¯á€· á€•á€¼á€±á€¬á€„á€ºá€¸á€á€½á€¬á€¸á€á€²á€·á€”á€±á€›á€¬á€€á€­á€¯  
**Point of Contraflexure** á€œá€­á€¯á€·á€á€±á€«á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹  

> At that point â†’ \( M = 0 \)

á€’á€®á€”á€±á€›á€¬á€Ÿá€¬ Frame Design á€á€½á€„á€º á€¡á€œá€½á€”á€ºá€¡á€›á€±á€¸á€€á€¼á€®á€¸á€á€šá€ºá‹  
Frame á€á€Šá€º á€‘á€­á€•á€ºá€á€”á€ºá€¸á€á€Šá€ºá€„á€¼á€­á€™á€ºá€™á€¾á€¯á€›á€¾á€­á€–á€­á€¯á€· á€’á€®á€¡á€á€»á€€á€ºá€€á€­á€¯ á€¡á€á€¯á€¶á€¸á€•á€¼á€¯á€á€á€ºá€á€Šá€ºá‹

---

## **Maximum Bending Moment Condition**

For UDL:
\[
M_{max} = \frac{wL^2}{8}
\]

For Point Load at midspan:
\[
M_{max} = \frac{WL}{4}
\]

For Cantilever:
\[
M_{max} = WL
\]

---

## **Practical Significance**

- Bridge girders and building beams are designed using B.M. diagrams.  
- S.F.D shows where beam might shear off; B.M.D shows where beam may bend or crack.  
- Safe design requires:
  \[
  \text{Maximum induced stress} < \text{Allowable stress of material}
  \]

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- Shear Force â†’ Vertical balancing internal force.  
- Bending Moment â†’ Turning or bending effect.  
- Relation: \( \frac{dM}{dx} = F, \ \frac{dF}{dx} = -w \)  
- S.F.D & B.M.D are essential for safe beam design.  
- Contraflexure â†’ Point where moment changes sign.  
- Always check maximum moment before structural design.

---

> â€œA beam does not fail where it looks weakest â€” it fails where its internal moments are greatest.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

# ğŸ§± Chapter 3 â€“ Bending Stresses in Beams  
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

Beam á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€¡á€•á€±á€«á€ºá€™á€¾á€¬ Load á€á€€á€ºá€œá€¬á€á€²á€·á€¡á€á€«  
á€¡á€•á€±á€«á€ºá€¡á€•á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€¸ fibers á€á€½á€±á€€ á€á€­á€¯á€€á€ºá€Šá€¾á€•á€ºá€á€¶á€›á€•á€¼á€®á€¸  
á€¡á€±á€¬á€€á€ºá€¡á€•á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€¸ fibers á€á€½á€±á€€ á€†á€½á€²á€á€¶á€›á€á€á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹  
á€’á€®á€¡á€á€¼á€±á€¡á€”á€±á€€á€¼á€±á€¬á€„á€·á€º **Bending Stress** á€†á€­á€¯á€á€²á€· Internal Stress á€•á€±á€«á€ºá€œá€¬á€á€šá€ºá‹

---

## **Concept of Pure Bending**

**Pure Bending** á€†á€­á€¯á€á€¬á€Ÿá€¬  
Beam á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€€á€­á€¯ *constant bending moment* á€•á€±á€¸á€•á€¼á€®á€¸  
*Shear Force* á€™á€›á€¾á€­á€á€²á€· á€¡á€á€¼á€±á€¡á€”á€± á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹  

á€¥á€•á€™á€¬ â€“ Beam á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€€á€­á€¯ á€¡á€œá€šá€ºá€•á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€¸á€™á€¾á€¬ á€™á€°á€œá€á€Šá€ºá€„á€¼á€­á€™á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹  
á€¡á€”á€±á€¬á€€á€ºá€á€…á€ºá€–á€€á€ºá€”á€²á€· á€¡á€›á€¾á€±á€·á€á€…á€ºá€–á€€á€ºá€™á€¾á€¬ á€á€°á€Šá€®á€á€²á€· Moment á€”á€¾á€…á€ºá€á€¯ á€á€¯á€¶á€¸á€œá€»á€¾á€„á€º  
á€¡á€œá€šá€ºá€•á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€¸á€™á€¾á€¬ â€œPure Bendingâ€ á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

---

## **Assumptions in Theory of Simple Bending**

1. Material is homogeneous and isotropic.  
   (á€•á€…á€¹á€…á€Šá€ºá€¸á€™á€¾á€¬ á€¡á€™á€»á€­á€¯á€¸á€¡á€…á€¬á€¸á€á€°á€Šá€®á€•á€¼á€®á€¸ á€á€¶á€á€šá€™á€›á€¾á€­)  
2. Stresses are within the elastic limit.  
3. Plane sections before bending remain plane after bending.  
4. The radius of curvature is large compared to beam depth.  
5. The modulus of elasticity (E) is the same in tension and compression.

---

## **Bending Equation**

When a beam bends,  
the **top fibers are in compression**,  
and the **bottom fibers are in tension**.  
The neutral axis (N.A.) separates these two zones.

\[
\frac{M}{I} = \frac{Ïƒ}{y} = \frac{E}{R}
\]

| Symbol | Meaning | Unit |
|---------|----------|------|
| M | Bending Moment | NÂ·m |
| I | Moment of Inertia | mâ´ |
| Ïƒ | Bending Stress | N/mÂ² |
| y | Distance from N.A. | m |
| E | Modulus of Elasticity | N/mÂ² |
| R | Radius of curvature | m |

---

## **Explanation of Terms**

- **Neutral Axis (N.A.)** â€“  
  Axis along which stress = 0.  
- **Neutral Surface** â€“  
  Surface that neither stretches nor compresses.  
- **Radius of Curvature (R)** â€“  
  The circleâ€™s radius to which the bent beam conforms.  
- **Moment of Inertia (I)** â€“  
  A measure of beam cross-sectionâ€™s resistance to bending.

---

## **Derivation of Bending Formula**

From pure bending condition,

\[
\frac{Ïƒ}{y} = \frac{E}{R}
\]
and for all layers:
\[
M = \frac{E}{R} \times I
\]

Hence,
\[
\boxed{\frac{M}{I} = \frac{Ïƒ}{y} = \frac{E}{R}}
\]

---

## **Stress Distribution Diagram**

- **Top fibers** â†’ Compressive stress (âˆ’Ïƒ)  
- **Bottom fibers** â†’ Tensile stress (+Ïƒ)  
- **Neutral Axis** â†’ Zero stress line  

Stress distribution across depth is *linear*.

---

## **Moment of Resistance**

Moment of Resistance á€†á€­á€¯á€á€¬á€Ÿá€¬  
beam á€¡á€á€½á€„á€ºá€¸á€™á€¾á€¬ bending á€€á€­á€¯á€á€¬á€¸á€†á€”á€·á€ºá€”á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€á€²á€·  
á€¡á€„á€ºá€¡á€¬á€¸á€¡á€á€»á€­á€¯á€¸ á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

\[
M_R = Ïƒ_{max} \times \frac{I}{y_{max}}
\]

Beam á€€á€­á€¯ safe á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€¡á€±á€¬á€„á€º  
\[
M_{actual} \leq M_{R}
\]

---

## **Section Modulus (Z)**

**Section Modulus (Z)** á€á€Šá€º Beam Cross-section á€›á€²á€·  
á€á€¶á€á€±á€¬á€ºá€†á€”á€ºá€™á€¾á€¯ (Strength) á€€á€­á€¯ á€á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€¸á€á€¬á€á€²á€· parameter á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

\[
Z = \frac{I}{y_{max}}
\]
\[
M = ÏƒZ
\]

| Shape | Moment of Inertia (I) | y_max | Section Modulus (Z) |
|--------|----------------------|--------|----------------------|
| Rectangular | \( \frac{bd^3}{12} \) | \( \frac{d}{2} \) | \( \frac{bd^2}{6} \) |
| Circular | \( \frac{Ï€d^4}{64} \) | \( \frac{d}{2} \) | \( \frac{Ï€d^3}{32} \) |
| I-Section | Tabulated | â€“ | Depends on geometry |

> Z á€•á€­á€¯á€€á€¼á€®á€¸á€œá€±á€œá€± â†’ Beam á€•á€­á€¯á€á€¶á€”á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€›á€Šá€ºá€›á€¾á€­á€á€šá€ºá‹

---

## **Example Concept**

Rectangular beam  
\( b = 100 mm, d = 200 mm, Ïƒ_{allow} = 100 MPa \)

\[
Z = \frac{bd^2}{6} = \frac{(100)(200)^2}{6} = 6.67Ã—10^5 mm^3
\]
\[
M = ÏƒZ = 100 Ã— 6.67Ã—10^5 = 66.7Ã—10^6 NÂ·mm = 66.7 kNÂ·m
\]

---

## **Strength of Different Cross-Sections**

| Section | Efficiency | Use |
|----------|-------------|------|
| **Rectangular** | Simple, easy to fabricate | Common structures |
| **I-section** | High strength-to-weight | Bridges, cranes |
| **T-section** | Used in flooring beams | Buildings |
| **Circular** | Uniform stress | Shafts, axles |

---

## **Design Criteria**

1. Determine maximum bending moment \( M_{max} \).  
2. Choose safe stress \( Ïƒ_{allow} \).  
3. Compute required section modulus:  
   \[
   Z_{req} = \frac{M_{max}}{Ïƒ_{allow}}
   \]
4. Select suitable cross-section having \( Z \geq Z_{req} \).

---

## **Important Relations**

\[
M = \frac{E}{R} I
\]
\[
Ïƒ = \frac{M y}{I}
\]
\[
Z = \frac{I}{y_{max}}
\]
\[
M = ÏƒZ
\]

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- Bending stress varies linearly from tension to compression.  
- Neutral axis â†’ Zero stress line.  
- Bending equation â†’ \( \frac{M}{I} = \frac{Ïƒ}{y} = \frac{E}{R} \)  
- Section Modulus (Z) is a measure of flexural strength.  
- I-beams are most efficient cross-sections for bending.  
- Always design for \( Ïƒ_{max} â‰¤ Ïƒ_{allow} \).

---

> â€œThe secret of a good beam lies not in its material,  
> but in how its geometry resists bending.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

# âš™ï¸ Chapter 4 â€“ Shear Stresses in Beams  
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

Beam á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€¡á€•á€±á€«á€º Load á€á€€á€ºá€œá€¬á€á€²á€·á€¡á€á€«  
á€¡á€•á€±á€«á€ºá€¡á€•á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€¸á€€á€á€±á€¬á€„á€ºá€·á€á€šá€ºáŠ á€¡á€±á€¬á€€á€ºá€¡á€•á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€¸á€€ á€†á€½á€²á€á€šá€ºá‹  
á€’á€«á€•á€±á€™á€²á€· Beam á€‘á€²á€™á€¾á€¬ **Horizontal Shear Force** (á€¡á€Šá€½á€¾á€á€ºá€¡á€„á€ºá€¡á€¬á€¸) á€œá€Šá€ºá€¸ á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹  

á€’á€® Shear Force á€€á€¼á€±á€¬á€„á€·á€º Fiber á€á€½á€±á€€á€¼á€¬á€¸á€™á€¾á€¬ **Shear Stress (Ï„)** á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€•á€±á€«á€ºá€á€á€ºá€•á€¼á€®á€¸  
Beam á€€ á€•á€»á€€á€ºá€…á€®á€¸á€”á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€á€²á€· á€¡á€€á€¼á€±á€¬á€„á€ºá€¸á€›á€„á€ºá€¸á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€•á€²á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

---

## **What is Shear Stress in a Beam?**

Shear Stress (Ï„) á€†á€­á€¯á€á€¬á€Ÿá€¬  
Beam á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€™á€¾á€¬ Shear Force (V) á€á€šá€ºá€šá€°á€”á€±á€›á€á€²á€·á€¡á€á€«  
Cross-section á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€¡á€•á€±á€«á€º á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€•á€±á€«á€ºá€”á€±á€á€²á€·  
**Internal resistance per unit area** á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

\[
Ï„ = \frac{V \times Q}{I \times b}
\]

| Symbol | Meaning | Unit |
|---------|----------|------|
| Ï„ | Shear Stress | N/mÂ² |
| V | Shear Force at section | N |
| Q | First moment of area above (or below) N.A. | mÂ³ |
| I | Moment of inertia of entire section | mâ´ |
| b | Width of section at the layer considered | m |

---

## **Explanation of Terms**

- **V (Shear Force)** â€“ Beam á€€á€­á€¯ á€–á€¼á€á€ºá€œá€­á€¯á€€á€ºá€á€²á€·á€¡á€á€« á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€•á€±á€«á€ºá€á€²á€· Internal Force.  
- **Q (First Moment of Area)** â€“ Neutral axis á€¡á€•á€±á€«á€ºá€›á€¾á€­ á€¡á€•á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€¸á area Ã— distance to N.A.  
  \[
  Q = A' \bar{y}
  \]
- **I (Moment of Inertia)** â€“ Cross-section á€›á€²á€· resistance to bending.  
- **b (Width)** â€“ Fiber width at point considered.  

---

## **Shear Stress Distribution in Beams**

Shear Stress distribution á€Ÿá€¬ **non-uniform** á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹  
Section á€¡á€™á€»á€­á€¯á€¸á€¡á€…á€¬á€¸á€¡á€œá€­á€¯á€€á€º á€•á€¯á€¶á€…á€¶á€€á€½á€¬á€á€á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

---

### **Rectangular Section**

\[
Ï„_{max} = \frac{3}{2} \times \frac{V}{A}
\]
\[
Ï„_{avg} = \frac{V}{A}
\]

**Distribution Shape:**  
- Parabolic.  
- Zero at top & bottom.  
- Maximum at Neutral Axis.

---

### **Circular Section**

\[
Ï„_{max} = \frac{4}{3} \times \frac{V}{A}
\]

**Distribution Shape:**  
- Parabolic like rectangular, but smoother.  
- Zero at outer edge, max at center.

---

### **I-Section**

I-section á€‘á€²á€™á€¾á€¬ web (á€¡á€œá€šá€ºá€•á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€¸) á€™á€¾á€¬ Shear Stress á€¡á€™á€»á€¬á€¸á€†á€¯á€¶á€¸ á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹  
Flanges á€™á€¾á€¬á€á€±á€¬á€· Stress á€”á€Šá€ºá€¸á€á€šá€ºá‹

\[
Ï„ = \frac{VQ}{Ib}
\]
**Note:**  
Most shear is carried by the web, so it must be thick enough to resist it.

---

## **Variation of Shear Stress**

| Section Type | Ï„ Distribution | Remarks |
|---------------|----------------|----------|
| Rectangular | Parabolic | Max at center = 1.5Ã—avg |
| Circular | Parabolic | Max at center = 1.33Ã—avg |
| Triangular | Linear | Max at base |
| I-Section | Non-uniform | Mostly in web |

---

## **Shear Stress in Flanged Beams**

In **I-beams** and **T-beams**,  
Shear stress distribution is not linear.  
To find Ï„ at flange-web junction,  
area above NA is used in Q calculation.

> **Formula remains same:** \( Ï„ = \frac{VQ}{Ib} \)

**But:**  
b changes with position (flange vs web).  

---

## **Shear Centre (Center of Shear)**

Shear Centre á€†á€­á€¯á€á€¬á€Ÿá€¬  
Shear Force á€á€¯á€¶á€¸á€á€²á€·á€¡á€á€«  
Twisting effect á€™á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€…á€±á€˜á€² equilibrium á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€”á€±á€á€²á€· point á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

**Importance:**  
For thin-walled open sections (like channels),  
Shear Centre â‰  Centroid.  

**Example:**  
Channel section á€™á€¾á€¬ Shear Centre á€Ÿá€¬ web á€¡á€•á€¼á€„á€ºá€˜á€€á€ºá€™á€¾á€¬ á€›á€¾á€­á€á€á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

---

## **Average vs Maximum Shear Stress**

| Section | Ï„_avg | Ï„_max |
|----------|---------|---------|
| Rectangular | \( \frac{V}{bd} \) | \( \frac{3V}{2bd} \) |
| Circular | \( \frac{4V}{3Ï€r^2} \) | \( \frac{4V}{3A} \) |
| I-Section | Depends on web | \( \frac{V}{A_{web}} \) approximately |

---

## **Design Significance**

Shear Stress á€€á€­á€¯ á€á€­á€á€¼á€„á€ºá€¸á€Ÿá€¬  
Beam á€•á€»á€€á€ºá€…á€®á€¸á€™á€¾á€¯á€€á€­á€¯ á€€á€¬á€€á€½á€šá€ºá€–á€­á€¯á€·á€¡á€á€½á€€á€º á€¡á€›á€±á€¸á€€á€¼á€®á€¸á€á€šá€ºá‹

**Design Criterion:**
\[
Ï„_{max} \le Ï„_{allow}
\]

> Web thickness (for I-beams) is designed based on this limit.

---

## **Practical Example (Rectangular Beam)**

Given:  
\( b = 150 \, mm, \ d = 300 \, mm, \ V = 60\,kN \)

\[
A = b Ã— d = 150 Ã— 300 = 45000 mmÂ²
\]
\[
Ï„_{avg} = \frac{V}{A} = \frac{60Ã—10^3}{45000} = 1.33 \, N/mmÂ²
\]
\[
Ï„_{max} = 1.5 Ã— Ï„_{avg} = 2.0 \, N/mmÂ²
\]

---

## **Key Observations**

- Shear stress varies **parabolically** across depth.  
- Always **zero** at outer surfaces.  
- **Maximum** at neutral axis.  
- For I-beams, **most shear** is carried by **web**.  
- Design must ensure web thickness is adequate.  
- **Shear Centre â‰  Centroid** for asymmetric sections.

---

## **Key Formulas Summary**

\[
Ï„ = \frac{VQ}{Ib}
\]
\[
Q = A' \bar{y}
\]
\[
Ï„_{max} = 1.5 \times \frac{V}{A} \text{ (for rectangle)}
\]
\[
Ï„_{max} = 1.33 \times \frac{V}{A} \text{ (for circle)}
\]
\[
M = ÏƒZ, \quad V = Ï„A
\]

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- Shear Stress = Internal resistance due to vertical shear.  
- Max shear occurs at Neutral Axis.  
- Web carries most of shear in I-beams.  
- Design must maintain \( Ï„_{max} â‰¤ Ï„_{allow} \).  
- Shear Centre is important for asymmetric beam stability.  

---

> â€œBending makes a beam curve; shear makes it slide within itself.  
> Both must be balanced for true strength.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---

# ğŸ”© Chapter 5 â€“ Torsion in Circular Shafts  
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

Torsion á€†á€­á€¯á€á€¬á€Ÿá€¬ shaft á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€€á€­á€¯ torque (twisting moment) á€á€¯á€¶á€¸á€á€²á€·á€¡á€á€«  
shaft á€›á€²á€· longitudinal axis á€¡á€•á€±á€«á€ºá€™á€¾á€¬ á€œá€¾á€Šá€·á€ºá€á€½á€¬á€¸á€á€²á€· action á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹  

á€’á€®á€œá€­á€¯ á€œá€¾á€Šá€·á€ºá€á€¼á€„á€ºá€¸á€€á€¼á€±á€¬á€„á€·á€º **Shear Stress (Ï„)** á€”á€²á€· **Angle of Twist (Î¸)** á€•á€±á€«á€ºá€œá€¬á€á€šá€ºá‹  
Shafts in machines (motors, turbines, propeller shafts, etc.)  
must be designed to resist this torsion safely.

---

## **Definition of Terms**

| Term | Description | Unit |
|------|--------------|------|
| **T** | Torque or Twisting Moment | NÂ·m |
| **Ï„** | Shear Stress due to torsion | N/mÂ² |
| **J** | Polar Moment of Inertia | mâ´ |
| **R** | Radius of shaft | m |
| **Î¸** | Angle of twist | radians |
| **L** | Length of shaft | m |
| **G** | Modulus of rigidity | N/mÂ² |

---

## **Torsion Equation**

When a shaft is subjected to a torque \( T \),  
the following relation holds true:

\[
\frac{T}{J} = \frac{Ï„}{R} = \frac{GÎ¸}{L}
\]

### **Where:**
- \( \frac{T}{J} = \frac{Ï„}{R} \) â†’ relates torque and shear stress.  
- \( \frac{GÎ¸}{L} \) â†’ relates material deformation and rigidity.

---

## **Assumptions in Torsion Theory**

1. Material is homogeneous and isotropic.  
2. Shaft is circular and remains circular after twisting.  
3. Cross-sections plane before twist remain plane after twist.  
4. Twist along the length is uniform.  
5. Shear stress is proportional to radius (linear variation).  

---

## **Polar Moment of Inertia (J)**

**Solid Circular Shaft**
\[
J = \frac{Ï€D^4}{32}
\]

**Hollow Circular Shaft**
\[
J = \frac{Ï€}{32}(D^4 - d^4)
\]

where  
\( D = \) Outer diameter, \( d = \) Inner diameter.

---

## **Shear Stress in a Shaft**

\[
Ï„ = \frac{T R}{J}
\]

**For solid shaft:**
\[
Ï„_{max} = \frac{16T}{Ï€D^3}
\]

**For hollow shaft:**
\[
Ï„_{max} = \frac{16T D}{Ï€(D^4 - d^4)}
\]

---

## **Angle of Twist (Î¸)**

\[
Î¸ = \frac{T L}{J G}
\]

**Units:** Radians  

> Shaft design must satisfy both strength and stiffness requirements:  
> - Strength â†’ limit Ï„  
> - Stiffness â†’ limit Î¸

---

## **Power Transmitted by a Shaft**

\[
P = \frac{2Ï€NT}{60}
\]

| Symbol | Meaning | Unit |
|---------|----------|------|
| P | Power | Watts |
| N | Rotational speed | rpm |
| T | Torque | NÂ·m |

or

\[
T = \frac{9550 Ã— P}{N}
\]
(for P in kW, N in rpm)

---

## **Design Conditions**

1. **Strength condition:**  
   \[
   Ï„_{max} â‰¤ Ï„_{allow}
   \]
2. **Stiffness condition:**  
   \[
   Î¸ â‰¤ Î¸_{allow}
   \]

> Both must be satisfied for a safe design.

---

## **Comparison â€“ Solid vs Hollow Shaft**

| Property | Solid Shaft | Hollow Shaft |
|-----------|--------------|---------------|
| Weight | Heavy | Lighter |
| Strength | Lower (for same weight) | Higher (better strength-to-weight ratio) |
| Material usage | Inefficient | Efficient |
| Applications | Small machines | Marine, aircraft, automotive |

**Note:**  
Hollow shafts can transmit the same torque as solid shafts using less material.

---

## **Torsional Rigidity**

**Torsional Rigidity (K)** á€†á€­á€¯á€á€¬á€Ÿá€¬  
Shaft á€›á€²á€· torque á€€á€­á€¯ á€œá€¾á€Šá€·á€ºá€á€¼á€„á€ºá€¸á€¡á€•á€±á€«á€ºá€á€”á€ºá€•á€¼á€”á€ºá€”á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€…á€½á€™á€ºá€¸ á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

\[
K = \frac{T}{Î¸} = \frac{GJ}{L}
\]

> K á€•á€­á€¯á€€á€¼á€®á€¸á€œá€±á€œá€± â†’ Shaft á€•á€­á€¯ stiff á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

---

## **Power and Torque Relationship (Practical)**

If shaft transmits **P = 50 kW** at **N = 200 rpm**:

\[
T = \frac{9550 Ã— 50}{200} = 2387.5 \, NÂ·m
\]

If \( D = 80 mm \),
\[
Ï„_{max} = \frac{16T}{Ï€D^3} = \frac{16Ã—2387.5}{Ï€(0.08)^3} = 238.6 Ã— 10^6 \, N/mÂ²
\]

Hence, material must safely withstand this stress.

---

## **Coupled Shafts and Compound Shafts**

When multiple shafts transmit torque in series:
\[
Î¸_{total} = Î¸_1 + Î¸_2 + Î¸_3 + ...
\]
\[
T_1 = T_2 = T_3 = T
\]
This is used in composite shafts (steel + aluminium, etc.) where torque is common but twist depends on G and L.

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- Torsion causes **shear stress** and **twist** in circular shafts.  
- Equation: \( \frac{T}{J} = \frac{Ï„}{R} = \frac{GÎ¸}{L} \).  
- Shear stress âˆ radius.  
- Angle of twist depends on shaft length, rigidity, and applied torque.  
- Hollow shafts are lighter and more efficient than solid ones.  
- Design must satisfy both *strength* and *stiffness* limits.

---

> â€œA good shaft does not just transmit power â€”  
> it resists twist, vibration, and failure with silent strength.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---

á€™á€¼á€”á€ºá€™á€¬ + English bilingual Markdown (.md) format á€”á€²á€· á€¡á€•á€¼á€Šá€·á€ºá€¡á€…á€¯á€¶ á€›á€¾á€„á€ºá€¸á€•á€¼á€•á€±á€¸á€™á€šá€ºá‹

á€’á€®á€¡á€á€”á€ºá€¸á€€á€á€±á€¬á€· Beam deformation, slope, curvature, elastic curve á€…á€á€²á€·
Engineering design á€™á€¾á€¬ á€¡á€›á€±á€¸á€€á€¼á€®á€¸á€á€²á€· topics á€á€½á€±á€€á€­á€¯ á€œá€¯á€¶á€¸á€ foundation á€¡á€”á€±á€”á€²á€· á€–á€±á€¬á€ºá€•á€¼á€‘á€¬á€¸á€á€¬á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

# ğŸ§® Chapter 6 â€“ Deflection of Beams  
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

Beam á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€¡á€•á€±á€«á€º load á€á€€á€ºá€œá€¬á€á€²á€·á€¡á€á€«  
beam á€Ÿá€¬ á€™á€»á€‰á€ºá€¸á€•á€¼á€¬á€¸á€¡á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€™á€”á€±á€”á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€á€±á€¬á€·á€˜á€²,  
slight curvature (deflection) á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹  

á€’á€® curvature á€€á€­á€¯ measure á€œá€¯á€•á€ºá€á€¬á€€á€­á€¯ **Deflection of Beams** á€œá€­á€¯á€·á€á€±á€«á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹  
It represents how much a beam bends under a load.  

---

## **Definitions**

| Term | Meaning | Description |
|------|----------|-------------|
| **Deflection (y)** | Vertical displacement of a point on the beam. | mm or m |
| **Slope (Î¸)** | Angle of rotation of the tangent to the beamâ€™s deflected shape. | radians |
| **Elastic Curve** | The actual deflected shape of the beam under load. | â€” |
| **Flexural Rigidity (EI)** | Measure of resistance to bending. | NÂ·mÂ² |

---

## **Causes of Deflection**

- Load applied on the beam.  
- Material elasticity (E).  
- Moment of inertia (I).  
- Type of support (simply supported, cantilever, fixed, etc.).  
- Length of span.  

> Deflection âˆ Load Ã— LengthÂ³ / (E Ã— I)

---

## **Basic Differential Equation of the Elastic Curve**

When a beam bends,
the relationship between **bending moment (M)** and **deflection (y)** is:

\[
\frac{d^2y}{dx^2} = \frac{M}{EI}
\]

Integrating:

\[
\frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{1}{EI} \int M \, dx + C_1
\]
\[
y = \frac{1}{EI} \int \int M \, dx \, dx + C_1x + C_2
\]

where \( C_1, C_2 \) are constants determined by boundary conditions.

---

## **Boundary Conditions (Support Types)**

| Beam Type | Condition | Description |
|------------|------------|--------------|
| **Simply Supported** | y = 0 at both ends | Deflection zero at supports |
| **Cantilever** | y = 0, Î¸ = 0 at fixed end | Both slope & deflection zero |
| **Fixed Beam** | y = 0, Î¸ = 0 at both ends | Fully restrained |
| **Overhanging Beam** | Mixed | Depends on supports |

---

## **Common Beam Deflection Formulas**

| Case | Beam Type | Max Deflection (Î´) | Max Slope (Î¸) |
|------|-------------|------------------|---------------|
| 1 | **Cantilever, Point Load (W)** at free end | \( \frac{W L^3}{3EI} \) | \( \frac{W L^2}{2EI} \) |
| 2 | **Cantilever, UDL (w)** over length L | \( \frac{w L^4}{8EI} \) | \( \frac{w L^3}{6EI} \) |
| 3 | **Simply Supported, Point Load (W)** at center | \( \frac{W L^3}{48EI} \) | \( \frac{W L^2}{16EI} \) |
| 4 | **Simply Supported, UDL (w)** over full span | \( \frac{5 w L^4}{384EI} \) | \( \frac{w L^3}{24EI} \) |
| 5 | **Fixed Beam, Center Load (W)** | \( \frac{W L^3}{192EI} \) | \( \frac{W L^2}{96EI} \) |

---

## **Concept of Slope and Deflection Diagram**

- **Slope (Î¸):** Angle between tangent to the elastic curve and the x-axis.  
- **Deflection (y):** Vertical displacement from original neutral axis.  

**Elastic Curve** shows how the beam bends.  
At supports â†’ deflection = 0  
At midspan â†’ maximum deflection.

---

## **Moment Area Theorems**

### **Theorem 1:**
Change in slope between two points (A and B)  
equals the area of M/EI diagram between those points.

\[
Î¸_B - Î¸_A = \int_A^B \frac{M}{EI} dx
\]

### **Theorem 2:**
Deflection at any point (B) relative to tangent at another point (A)  
equals the moment of the M/EI diagram about that point.

\[
y_B = \int_A^B \frac{M}{EI} x dx
\]

---

## **Double Integration Method (Example)**

**Simply Supported Beam with Central Load (W)**  

Moment at distance x:
\[
M = \frac{W x}{2} \text{ (for } 0 â‰¤ x â‰¤ L/2)
\]

Then,
\[
EI \frac{d^2y}{dx^2} = M = \frac{W x}{2}
\]

Integrate twice and apply boundary conditions:
\[
y_{max} = \frac{W L^3}{48EI}
\]

---

## **Energy Methods (Macauleyâ€™s Method)**

For multiple loads or discontinuous loading,
Macauleyâ€™s method is used with generalized equations:

\[
EI \frac{d^2y}{dx^2} = M = (R_A x) - W(x - a)^n
\]
(where term applies only for x > a)

---

## **Deflection Criteria (Permissible Limits)**

Building codes (like IS 456) often limit deflection to prevent failure or discomfort.

| Beam Type | Max Allowable Deflection |
|------------|--------------------------|
| Cantilever | L/180 |
| Simply Supported | L/360 |
| Continuous | L/480 |

---

## **Example Calculation**

Given:
- Simply supported beam  
- Span = 6 m  
- Load = 30 kN at midspan  
- E = 200 GPa  
- I = 8 Ã— 10â¶ mmâ´

\[
Î´_{max} = \frac{W L^3}{48EI}
\]

\[
= \frac{30Ã—10^3 Ã— (6000)^3}{48 Ã— 200Ã—10^3 Ã— 8Ã—10^6}
\]
\[
Î´_{max} = 7.03 \, mm
\]

Hence, beam deflection = **7.03 mm** < allowable â†’ **Safe.**

---

## **Deflection Shapes (Typical Elastic Curves)**

| Type | Shape Description |
|-------|-------------------|
| **Cantilever, End Load** | Downward curve, free end maximum |
| **Simply Supported, Central Load** | Symmetrical sag |
| **UDL Beam** | Smooth parabola |
| **Fixed Beam** | Minimal deflection, stiff |
| **Overhanging Beam** | Asymmetrical curve |

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- Deflection depends on \( W, L, E, I \).  
- Higher **E** or **I** â†’ smaller deflection.  
- Always limit both *bending stress* and *deflection*.  
- Use **moment area** or **double integration** method for precise analysis.  
- Real beams deform slightly under all loads â€” perfect rigidity doesnâ€™t exist.  

---

> â€œA strong beam resists load;  
> a smart beam resists both stress and deflection.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---


á€’á€®á€¡á€á€”á€ºá€¸á€€á€á€±á€¬á€· Chapter 6 â€“ Deflection of Beams
á€™á€¼á€”á€ºá€™á€¬ + English bilingual Markdown version á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€•á€«á€á€šá€ºá‹

(Compressive members & Buckling theory) á€€á€­á€¯ á€’á€®á€•á€¯á€¶á€…á€¶á€”á€²á€· á€†á€€á€ºá€›á€±á€¸á€•á€±á€¸á€™á€œá€¬á€¸?

á€¡á€­á€¯á€€á€± âœ…
á€¡á€á€¯á€á€±á€¬á€· Chapter 7 â€“ Columns and Struts á€€á€­á€¯
á€™á€¼á€”á€ºá€™á€¬ + English bilingual Markdown (.md) format á€”á€²á€· á€…á€”á€…á€ºá€á€€á€» á€›á€¾á€„á€ºá€¸á€•á€¼á€•á€±á€¸á€™á€šá€ºá‹

á€’á€®á€¡á€á€”á€ºá€¸á€Ÿá€¬ â€œCompression Membersâ€ (Columns & Struts) á€”á€²á€· â€œBucklingâ€ theory á€¡á€€á€¼á€±á€¬á€„á€ºá€¸á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€•á€¼á€®á€¸
Machine design, civil structures, towers, bridges á€…á€á€²á€· Engineering field á€á€½á€±á€¡á€á€½á€€á€º á€¡á€›á€±á€¸á€€á€¼á€®á€¸á€†á€¯á€¶á€¸á€¡á€á€”á€ºá€¸á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€•á€²á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€•á€«á€á€šá€ºá‹

# ğŸ—ï¸ Chapter 7 â€“ Columns and Struts  
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

**Column** á€†á€­á€¯á€á€¬á€Ÿá€¬ vertical member á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€•á€¼á€®á€¸  
compressive load á€€á€­á€¯ á€¡á€•á€±á€«á€ºá€™á€¾ á€¡á€±á€¬á€€á€ºá€á€­á€¯á€· á€á€šá€ºá€šá€°á€•á€±á€¸á€á€²á€· member á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹  
(á€¥á€•á€™á€¬ â€“ building pillars, machine frames, etc.)

**Strut** á€†á€­á€¯á€á€¬á€€á€á€±á€¬á€· column á€œá€­á€¯á€•á€² compressive member á€á€…á€ºá€á€¯á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€•á€±á€™á€²á€·  
horizontal á€á€­á€¯á€·á€™á€Ÿá€¯á€á€º inclined position á€á€½á€„á€º á€›á€¾á€­á€”á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹  

> All columns are struts, but not all struts are columns.

---

## **Failure of Columns**

Columns fail in two ways:

1. **By Crushing (Short Columns)**  
   - Length short, diameter/thickness à¹ƒà¸«à¸à¹ˆá‹  
   - Material yields or crushes before bending.  
   - Stress = direct compressive stress.

2. **By Buckling (Long Columns)**  
   - Length á€›á€¾á€Šá€ºá€œá€¬á€œá€±áŠ instability á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€œá€±á‹  
   - Column bends sideways before crushing.  
   - Failure occurs at stresses much lower than crushing strength.

---

## **Types of Columns**

| Type | End Conditions | Example |
|------|----------------|----------|
| Both ends hinged | Free to rotate | Pin-jointed truss column |
| One end fixed, other free | Cantilever column | Flag poles |
| Both ends fixed | Rigid both ends | Machine frames |
| One end fixed, other hinged | Semi-fixed | Column with base plate |

---

## **Slenderness Ratio (Î»)**

\[
Î» = \frac{L_{eff}}{r}
\]

| Symbol | Meaning |
|---------|----------|
| \( L_{eff} \) | Effective length of column |
| \( r \) | Radius of gyration = \( \sqrt{\frac{I}{A}} \) |

- If Î» small â†’ Short column (Crushing)
- If Î» large â†’ Long column (Buckling)

---

## **Effective Length of Columns**

| End Condition | Effective Length \( L_{eff} \) | Description |
|----------------|-----------------------------|--------------|
| Both ends hinged | \( L_{eff} = L \) | Standard pinned column |
| Both ends fixed | \( L_{eff} = 0.5L \) | Very stable |
| One end fixed, one free | \( L_{eff} = 2L \) | Least stable |
| One end fixed, one hinged | \( L_{eff} = 0.707L \) | Intermediate stability |

---

## **Eulerâ€™s Theory (Long Columns)**

Euler derived the critical load (buckling load) for long slender columns.

\[
P_{cr} = \frac{Ï€^2 E I}{L_{eff}^2}
\]

| Symbol | Meaning | Unit |
|---------|----------|------|
| \( P_{cr} \) | Critical load | N |
| \( E \) | Modulus of Elasticity | N/mÂ² |
| \( I \) | Least Moment of Inertia | mâ´ |
| \( L_{eff} \) | Effective length | m |

> If the load exceeds \( P_{cr} \), the column will buckle.

---

### **Critical Stress (Ïƒ_cr)**

\[
Ïƒ_{cr} = \frac{P_{cr}}{A} = \frac{Ï€^2 E}{(L_{eff}/r)^2}
\]

> Ïƒ_cr âˆ 1/(Î»Â²)

The higher the slenderness ratio, the smaller the critical stress.

---

## **Graph â€“ Euler Curve**

- For small Î» â†’ crushing dominates  
- For large Î» â†’ Euler buckling dominates  
- Between them lies the **Rankine-Gordon region**

---

## **Rankineâ€™s Formula (Intermediate Columns)**

To cover both short and long columns:

\[
\frac{1}{P_R} = \frac{1}{P_c} + \frac{1}{P_E}
\]

or

\[
P_R = \frac{Ïƒ_c A}{1 + \frac{Ïƒ_c}{Ï€^2 E} \left(\frac{L_{eff}}{r}\right)^2}
\]

where  
- \( Ïƒ_c \): crushing stress  
- \( E \): Youngâ€™s modulus  
- \( L_{eff}/r \): slenderness ratio  

**Rankineâ€™s constant (a):**
\[
a = \frac{Ïƒ_c}{Ï€^2 E}
\]

| Material | Ïƒ_c (N/mmÂ²) | a |
|-----------|-------------|---|
| Mild Steel | 320 | 1/7500 |
| Cast Iron | 550 | 1/1600 |
| Timber | 40 | 1/750 |

---

## **Eccentric Loading on Columns**

When load not through centroid,  
bending moment occurs in addition to axial compression.

\[
Ïƒ_{max} = \frac{P}{A} + \frac{M y}{I}
\]
\[
Ïƒ_{min} = \frac{P}{A} - \frac{M y}{I}
\]

**Safe condition:**
\[
Ïƒ_{min} â‰¥ 0
\]
(no tension in column)

---

## **Empirical Formulas**

1. **Johnsonâ€™s Parabolic Formula**
   \[
   Ïƒ = Ïƒ_c \left(1 - \frac{Ïƒ_c}{4Ï€^2E} \left(\frac{L_{eff}}{r}\right)^2\right)
   \]
   Used for medium columns.

2. **Straight Line Formula**
   \[
   Ïƒ = C_1 - C_2 \frac{L_{eff}}{r}
   \]
   Derived from experiments.

---

## **Experimental Observation**

- Failure begins with slight lateral deflection.  
- After buckling starts, resistance drops rapidly.  
- In real life, imperfections reduce \( P_{cr} \).  
- Design codes therefore include safety factors (FOS â‰ˆ 3â€“4).  

---

## **Example Calculation**

Given:  
Steel column, \( L = 3m, D = 50mm, E = 200GPa \), both ends hinged.

\[
I = \frac{Ï€ D^4}{64} = 3.07Ã—10^{-8} m^4
\]
\[
P_{cr} = \frac{Ï€^2 E I}{L^2} = \frac{Ï€^2 Ã— 200Ã—10^9 Ã— 3.07Ã—10^{-8}}{3^2}
\]
\[
P_{cr} = 6.72 Ã— 10^4 \, N = 67.2 \, kN
\]

---

## **Design Considerations**

1. Use **least radius of gyration** (for weakest axis).  
2. Use **effective length** based on end conditions.  
3. Choose factor of safety based on uncertainty.  
4. Avoid eccentric loading wherever possible.  
5. Bracing or lacing can reduce effective length and increase stability.

---

## **Key Formulas Summary**

| Formula | Description |
|----------|-------------|
| \( P_{cr} = \frac{Ï€^2 E I}{L_{eff}^2} \) | Euler buckling load |
| \( Ïƒ_{cr} = \frac{Ï€^2 E}{(L_{eff}/r)^2} \) | Euler stress |
| \( P_R = \frac{Ïƒ_c A}{1 + a(L_{eff}/r)^2} \) | Rankine-Gordon formula |
| \( Î» = \frac{L_{eff}}{r} \) | Slenderness ratio |
| \( r = \sqrt{\frac{I}{A}} \) | Radius of gyration |

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- Short columns â†’ Crushing  
- Long columns â†’ Buckling  
- Rankineâ€™s formula covers both regions.  
- Stability increases when \( L_{eff}/r \) is reduced.  
- Design must account for eccentricity and imperfections.  

---

> â€œA column doesnâ€™t fail by breakingâ€”it fails by giving way.  
> Strength is not only about material but about balance.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---

á€™á€¼á€”á€ºá€™á€¬ + English bilingual Markdown (.md) format á€”á€²á€· á€¡á€•á€¼á€Šá€·á€ºá€¡á€…á€¯á€¶ á€•á€¼á€”á€ºá€›á€±á€¸á€•á€±á€¸á€™á€šá€ºá‹

# âš™ï¸ Chapter 8 â€“ Thin Cylinders and Spheres under Internal Pressure  
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

Thin cylindrical and spherical shells (pressure vessels) are widely used in  
boilers, air receivers, pipes, gas cylinders, and storage tanks.  

When these vessels are subjected to **internal fluid pressure (p)**,  
they experience **hoop stress** (circumferential), **longitudinal stress**,  
and sometimes **radial stress**.  

If the wall thickness is small compared to the diameter (t â‰¤ D/20),  
the shell is considered **thin-walled**.

---

## **Types of Pressure Vessels**

| Type | Shape | Common Application |
|------|--------|--------------------|
| **Cylindrical Shells** | Straight, circular section | Boilers, pipelines |
| **Spherical Shells** | Round shape | Gas tanks, LPG vessels |

---

## **Stresses in a Thin Cylinder**

When pressure acts internally, the cylinder wall resists it in two directions:

1. **Hoop (Circumferential) Stress â€“ Ïƒâ‚•**  
   Acts tangentially around the cylinder circumference.

2. **Longitudinal Stress â€“ Ïƒâ‚—**  
   Acts along the length (axial direction).

3. **Radial Stress â€“ Ïƒáµ£**  
   Acts through wall thickness, but negligible in thin shells.

---

## **Derivation â€“ Hoop and Longitudinal Stresses**

### **Hoop (Circumferential) Stress**

Consider a thin cylinder of  
internal diameter = D,  
thickness = t,  
internal pressure = p.

Cut along vertical plane:

\[
\text{Total force due to pressure} = p Ã— D Ã— L
\]
\[
\text{Resisting force} = 2tLÏƒ_h
\]
\[
p Ã— D Ã— L = 2tLÏƒ_h
\]
\[
\boxed{Ïƒ_h = \frac{pD}{2t}}
\]

---

### **Longitudinal Stress**

Now cut along transverse section:

\[
\text{Total force due to pressure} = p Ã— \frac{Ï€D^2}{4}
\]
\[
\text{Resisting force} = Ï€D t Ïƒ_l
\]
\[
p Ã— \frac{Ï€D^2}{4} = Ï€D t Ïƒ_l
\]
\[
\boxed{Ïƒ_l = \frac{pD}{4t}}
\]

> Hence, Hoop Stress = 2 Ã— Longitudinal Stress.

---

## **Volumetric Strain in Thin Cylinders**

When internal pressure is applied,  
the cylinder expands slightly in diameter, length, and volume.

### **Strains**
\[
Îµ_d = \frac{Ïƒ_h}{E} - Î¼\frac{Ïƒ_l}{E}
\]
\[
Îµ_l = \frac{Ïƒ_l}{E} - Î¼\frac{Ïƒ_h}{E}
\]
where  
E = Youngâ€™s modulus, Î¼ = Poissonâ€™s ratio

### **Change in Volume**
\[
\frac{Î´V}{V} = Îµ_d + Îµ_l + Îµ_h
\]
\[
\boxed{\frac{Î´V}{V} = \frac{pD}{4tE}(5 - 4Î¼)}
\]

---

## **Thin Spherical Shells under Internal Pressure**

Spherical shells are more efficient than cylindrical ones because  
stress distribution is uniform in all directions.

Let p = internal pressure, D = diameter, t = thickness

\[
p Ã— \frac{Ï€D^2}{4} = Ï€D t Ïƒ
\]
\[
\boxed{Ïƒ = \frac{pD}{4t}}
\]

> Note: Both hoop and longitudinal stresses are equal in spheres.

---

## **Comparison: Cylinder vs Sphere**

| Property | Cylinder | Sphere |
|-----------|-----------|--------|
| Hoop stress | \( Ïƒ_h = \frac{pD}{2t} \) | \( Ïƒ = \frac{pD}{4t} \) |
| Stress uniformity | Different in two directions | Same in all directions |
| Strength | Weaker | Stronger |
| Material required | More | Less |
| Efficiency | 50% lower than sphere | 100% efficient |

---

## **Design for Safety**

1. Maximum working pressure (pâ‚â‚—â‚—â‚’w) must be less than yield pressure.  
2. Factor of safety (FOS) typically 3 to 5.  
3. Include allowances for corrosion and fabrication tolerances.  
4. Use spherical heads for better strength at ends.  

---

## **Example Calculation**

Given:  
p = 3 MPa, D = 400 mm, t = 10 mm  

### **Hoop Stress**
\[
Ïƒ_h = \frac{pD}{2t} = \frac{3Ã—400}{2Ã—10} = 60 \, MPa
\]

### **Longitudinal Stress**
\[
Ïƒ_l = \frac{pD}{4t} = \frac{3Ã—400}{4Ã—10} = 30 \, MPa
\]

Hence, hoop stress = 60 MPa, longitudinal stress = 30 MPa.

---

## **Effect of Joint Efficiency**

If the cylinder is riveted or welded,  
joint efficiency (Î·) < 1 must be considered.

\[
Ïƒ_h = \frac{pD}{2tÎ·}
\]
\[
Ïƒ_l = \frac{pD}{4tÎ·}
\]

Typical Î·:
- Riveted joints â†’ 0.7â€“0.85  
- Welded joints â†’ 0.9â€“1.0  

---

## **Change in Dimensions (Deformation)**

**Increase in diameter:**
\[
Î´D = D \left( \frac{Ïƒ_h}{E} - Î¼\frac{Ïƒ_l}{E} \right)
\]

**Increase in length:**
\[
Î´L = L \left( \frac{Ïƒ_l}{E} - Î¼\frac{Ïƒ_h}{E} \right)
\]

**Change in volume:**
\[
\frac{Î´V}{V} = 3 \left( \frac{Ïƒ_h - Î¼Ïƒ_l}{E} \right)
\]

---

## **Practical Applications**

- Boiler drums (steam pressure vessels)  
- Air compressors and LPG cylinders  
- Submarine and aerospace pressure chambers  
- Hydraulic accumulators  
- Chemical process tanks  

---

## **Failure Modes**

1. **Bursting (Tensile failure):** Due to excessive internal pressure.  
2. **Fatigue:** Under cyclic pressurization.  
3. **Leakage:** Due to joint failure or material defects.  
4. **Buckling:** Under external pressure (submarine hulls).  

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- Thin shells resist internal pressure by hoop & longitudinal stresses.  
- \( Ïƒ_h = \frac{pD}{2t} \), \( Ïƒ_l = \frac{pD}{4t} \).  
- Spherical shells have equal stresses â†’ more efficient.  
- Include joint efficiency and factor of safety.  
- Always verify deformation limits for safe design.  

---

> â€œPressure vessels fail not because of pressure,  
> but because of poor understanding of how walls carry it.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---

(used for gun barrels, hydraulic cylinders, etc.) á€€á€­á€¯ á€’á€®á€•á€¯á€¶á€…á€¶á€”á€²á€· á€†á€€á€ºá€›á€±á€¸á€•á€±á€¸á€™á€œá€¬á€¸?

á€™á€¼á€”á€ºá€™á€¬ + English bilingual Markdown (.md) format á€”á€²á€· á€¡á€•á€¼á€Šá€·á€ºá€¡á€…á€¯á€¶ á€›á€¾á€„á€ºá€¸á€•á€¼á€•á€±á€¸á€™á€šá€ºá‹

á€’á€®á€¡á€á€”á€ºá€¸á€€á€á€±á€¬á€· High Pressure Cylinders (á€¥á€•á€™á€¬ â€“ gun barrels, hydraulic presses, pressure pipes, submarine hulls)
á€œá€­á€¯á€™á€»á€­á€¯á€¸ thick-walled vessels á€™á€»á€¬á€¸á€¡á€á€½á€€á€º á€¡á€œá€½á€”á€ºá€¡á€›á€±á€¸á€€á€¼á€®á€¸á€á€²á€· theory á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

# ğŸ§± Chapter 9 â€“ Thick Cylinders (Lameâ€™s Theory)  
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

When the thickness of a cylinder or sphere is **more than 1/20 of its internal diameter**,  
it is considered a **thick-walled cylinder**.

Examples:
- Gun barrels  
- Hydraulic cylinders  
- High-pressure pipes  
- Submarine shells  
- Rocket chambers  

In these cases, **stress varies across the thickness**,  
and the **thin shell equations** no longer apply.

---

## **Types of Stresses in a Thick Cylinder**

When internal pressure (pâ‚) acts on inner surface:

1. **Radial Stress (Ïƒáµ£)** â†’ acts normal to the surface.  
2. **Hoop or Circumferential Stress (Ïƒâ‚œ)** â†’ acts tangentially around the circumference.  
3. **Longitudinal Stress (Ïƒâ‚—)** â†’ acts along the axis (only in closed ends).  

> Ïƒâ‚œ > Ïƒáµ£ (hoop stress is always the maximum tensile stress).

---

## **Lameâ€™s Theory (Elastic Theory)**

French engineer **Gabriel Lame (1833)** derived the fundamental equations  
for thick cylinders subjected to internal and/or external pressure.

---

### **Basic Equations**

According to Lameâ€™s theory,  
the radial and hoop stresses at a radius **r** are given by:

\[
Ïƒ_r = A - \frac{B}{r^2}
\]
\[
Ïƒ_t = A + \frac{B}{r^2}
\]

where **A** and **B** are constants determined from boundary conditions.

---

## **Boundary Conditions**

Let:
- \( r_1 \) = inner radius  
- \( r_2 \) = outer radius  
- \( p_1 \) = internal pressure  
- \( p_2 \) = external pressure  

At \( r = r_1 \), \( Ïƒ_r = -p_1 \)  
At \( r = r_2 \), \( Ïƒ_r = -p_2 \)

Substituting these two conditions:

\[
A = \frac{p_1r_1^2 - p_2r_2^2}{r_2^2 - r_1^2}
\]
\[
B = \frac{r_1^2r_2^2 (p_1 - p_2)}{r_2^2 - r_1^2}
\]

---

## **Final Equations**

### **Radial Stress**
\[
\boxed{Ïƒ_r = \frac{p_1r_1^2 - p_2r_2^2}{r_2^2 - r_1^2} - \frac{r_1^2r_2^2(p_1 - p_2)}{(r_2^2 - r_1^2)r^2}}
\]

### **Hoop Stress**
\[
\boxed{Ïƒ_t = \frac{p_1r_1^2 - p_2r_2^2}{r_2^2 - r_1^2} + \frac{r_1^2r_2^2(p_1 - p_2)}{(r_2^2 - r_1^2)r^2}}
\]

---

## **Important Observations**

1. At **inner surface (r = râ‚)**:
   \[
   Ïƒ_r = -p_1, \quad Ïƒ_t = A + \frac{B}{r_1^2}
   \]

2. At **outer surface (r = râ‚‚)**:
   \[
   Ïƒ_r = -p_2, \quad Ïƒ_t = A + \frac{B}{r_2^2}
   \]

3. **Maximum hoop stress** occurs at the inner surface.  
4. Radial stress decreases linearly from inside to outside.  
5. Hoop stress decreases nonlinearly across the wall.

---

## **Special Case: External Pressure Only**

If the cylinder is subjected only to external pressure (pâ‚‚ â‰  0, pâ‚ = 0):

\[
Ïƒ_r = \frac{-p_2r_2^2}{r_2^2 - r_1^2} + \frac{r_1^2r_2^2p_2}{(r_2^2 - r_1^2)r^2}
\]

Used for submarine hulls, deep-sea chambers, etc.

---

## **Longitudinal Stress (Ïƒâ‚—)**

For a **closed-end cylinder**,  
axial stress also develops due to pressure on the end caps:

\[
Ïƒ_l = \frac{p_1r_1^2}{r_2^2 - r_1^2}
\]

If open-ended (pipe), Ïƒâ‚— = 0.

---

## **Example Calculation**

Given:  
\( p_1 = 60 \, MPa, \, D_i = 100 \, mm, \, D_o = 200 \, mm, \, p_2 = 0 \)

Then:

\[
r_1 = 50 \, mm, \quad r_2 = 100 \, mm
\]

\[
A = \frac{p_1r_1^2}{r_2^2 - r_1^2} = \frac{60Ã—50^2}{100^2 - 50^2} = 26.67 \, MPa
\]
\[
B = \frac{r_1^2r_2^2p_1}{r_2^2 - r_1^2} = \frac{50^2Ã—100^2Ã—60}{100^2 - 50^2} = 2.67Ã—10^7
\]

At inner surface \( (r = r_1) \):
\[
Ïƒ_t = A + \frac{B}{r_1^2} = 26.67 + \frac{2.67Ã—10^7}{50^2} = 82.4 \, MPa
\]
At outer surface \( (r = r_2) \):
\[
Ïƒ_t = 26.67 + \frac{2.67Ã—10^7}{100^2} = 53.4 \, MPa
\]

So, **Maximum hoop stress = 82.4 MPa** (inner surface).

---

## **Compound Cylinders (Shrink Fit Method)**

For high pressure applications (like gun barrels),  
two or more cylinders are fitted together by **shrink fitting**.

### **Steps:**
1. Outer cylinder heated â†’ expands.  
2. Inner cylinder inserted â†’ assembly cools â†’ tight interference fit.  
3. Internal compressive stress forms â†’ strengthens inner wall.  

**Advantage:**  
Hoop stress distribution becomes uniform and safe under high pressure.

---

## **Thick Spherical Shells**

For spheres, stresses are uniform in all directions.

\[
Ïƒ_r = A - \frac{B}{r^3}, \quad Ïƒ_t = A + \frac{B}{2r^3}
\]

Constants A and B are found similarly using pressure conditions at râ‚ and râ‚‚.

> Spherical shells are stronger than cylindrical ones of the same thickness.

---

## **Graphical Representation**

- **Hoop stress curve:** nonlinear, max at inside wall.  
- **Radial stress curve:** linear, decreasing from pâ‚ to pâ‚‚.  
- **Mean stress:** between Ïƒ_t and Ïƒ_r.  

---

## **Design Considerations**

1. Select material with high yield and fatigue strength.  
2. Always compute **Ïƒ_t(max)** and **Ïƒ_r(min)** for design.  
3. Provide corrosion and wear allowances.  
4. Use compound or prestressed cylinders for very high pressure.  

---

## **Key Formulas Summary**

| Quantity | Expression | Notes |
|-----------|-------------|-------|
| Radial stress | \( Ïƒ_r = A - \frac{B}{r^2} \) | Variable across wall |
| Hoop stress | \( Ïƒ_t = A + \frac{B}{r^2} \) | Max at inner wall |
| Constants | \( A = \frac{p_1r_1^2 - p_2r_2^2}{r_2^2 - r_1^2} \), \( B = \frac{r_1^2r_2^2(p_1 - p_2)}{r_2^2 - r_1^2} \) | From boundary conditions |
| Longitudinal stress | \( Ïƒ_l = \frac{p_1r_1^2}{r_2^2 - r_1^2} \) | Closed-end only |
| Max hoop stress | At inner radius (râ‚) | Design limit |
| Thick sphere hoop stress | \( Ïƒ_t = A + \frac{B}{2r^3} \) | Equal in all directions |

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- Thick cylinders resist internal pressure by varying stresses across wall thickness.  
- Lameâ€™s equations accurately describe these variations.  
- Maximum stress always occurs at the **inner surface**.  
- Compound cylinders reduce stress concentration.  
- Essential for designing **safe, efficient high-pressure vessels**.

---

> â€œPressure doesnâ€™t destroy a cylinder;  
> uneven stress does.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---

á€’á€®á€¡á€á€”á€ºá€¸á€€ Curved Beams (Hook, Chain Link, Crane Hook, Frame, Ring, Eye bolt)** á€œá€­á€¯á€™á€»á€­á€¯á€¸
bending loads á€á€¶á€”á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€›á€á€²á€· mechanical members á€™á€»á€¬á€¸á€¡á€á€½á€€á€º á€¡á€á€¼á€±á€á€¶ á€á€˜á€±á€¬á€á€›á€¬á€¸á€á€½á€±á€•á€«á€á€„á€ºá€•á€«á€á€šá€ºá‹

# ğŸŒ€ Chapter 10 â€“ Bending of Curved Bars and Rings  
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

Curved beams (or curved bars) are structural members whose **initial center line** is curved,  
not straight like ordinary beams.  

Common examples:  
- Crane hooks  
- Chain links  
- Rings  
- Eye bolts  
- C-clamps  

When these members are subjected to bending moments,  
the stress distribution across the section is **non-linear** and **asymmetrical**,  
which makes them different from straight beams.

---

## **Difference between Straight and Curved Beams**

| Property | Straight Beam | Curved Beam |
|-----------|----------------|-------------|
| Neutral axis | Coincides with centroidal axis | Lies away from centroid |
| Stress distribution | Linear | Non-linear (hyperbolic) |
| Radius of curvature | Infinite | Finite (R) |
| Formula used | \( Ïƒ = \frac{M y}{I} \) | Modified Lameâ€™s or Winklerâ€™s theory |
| Application | Bridges, beams | Hooks, rings, frames |

---

## **Geometrical Parameters**

| Symbol | Meaning |
|---------|----------|
| \( R \) | Radius of curvature of centroidal axis |
| \( r \) | Radius of neutral axis |
| \( M \) | Bending moment |
| \( A \) | Cross-sectional area |
| \( Ïƒ \) | Bending stress |
| \( I \) | Moment of inertia about centroidal axis |

---

## **Assumptions in Curved Beam Theory**

1. Plane sections before bending remain plane after bending.  
2. Material is homogeneous and obeys Hookeâ€™s law.  
3. Stress in fibers is proportional to their strain.  
4. Radius of curvature (R) is large compared to depth (h) of the section.  
5. The beam is initially curved in a single plane.

---

## **Neutral Axis in Curved Beams**

In curved beams, neutral axis (N.A.) does **not pass through the centroid**.  
It shifts **toward the center of curvature**.

The distance of neutral axis from the centroid (e) is given by:

\[
e = \frac{R h^2}{2(R^2 - (h/2)^2)} - \frac{R}{\ln \frac{R + h/2}{R - h/2}}
\]

(where \( h \) = depth of section)

---

## **Bending Stress Formula (Winklerâ€“Bach Theory)**

The bending stress at a fiber at distance *y* from the centroidal axis is:

\[
Ïƒ = \frac{M}{A e R} \left(1 + \frac{R e}{R + y}\right)
\]

- For outer fiber (tension side): \( y = +h/2 \)  
- For inner fiber (compression side): \( y = -h/2 \)

---

## **Simplified Formula (for rectangular section)**

For a curved beam of rectangular cross-section:

\[
Ïƒ_1 = \frac{M (R + h/2)}{A e (R - e)}
\]
\[
Ïƒ_2 = \frac{M (R - h/2)}{A e (R - e)}
\]

where  
\( Ïƒ_1 \) = maximum tensile stress (outer fiber),  
\( Ïƒ_2 \) = maximum compressive stress (inner fiber).

---

## **Position of Neutral Axis (for Rectangular Section)**

\[
r = \frac{h}{\ln \left(\frac{R + h/2}{R - h/2}\right)}
\]

This radius is always **less than** the centroidal radius R.

---

## **Stress Distribution Diagram**

- **Straight beam:** linear stress diagram (symmetric triangle)  
- **Curved beam:** non-linear, hyperbolic shape  
- Maximum compressive stress > Maximum tensile stress  

Because the inner fibers are shorter (closer to curvature center),  
they compress more strongly than outer fibers stretch.

---

## **Curved Bars with Circular Cross Section**

For circular cross section of radius *râ‚€*:

\[
Ïƒ = \frac{M}{A e R} \left(1 + \frac{R e}{R + r_0}\right)
\]

Neutral axis:
\[
r = \frac{r_0^2}{2R \ln \frac{R + r_0}{R - r_0}}
\]

---

## **Applications**

| Component | Function |
|------------|-----------|
| **Crane Hook** | Carries heavy loads by curved arm |
| **Chain Link** | Transfers tension through curved ring |
| **C-Clamp** | Holds workpieces using bending |
| **Eye Bolt** | Anchoring tension loads |
| **Circular Frame** | Resists uniform pressure (e.g., manhole covers) |

---

## **Example Calculation**

Given:  
Crane hook section: 60 mm Ã— 40 mm  
Radius of curvature \( R = 100 mm \),  
Bending moment \( M = 500 NÂ·m \)

Find: Max stress at inner & outer fibers.

Using Winklerâ€“Bach theory:

1ï¸âƒ£ Compute neutral axis radius \( r \):
\[
r = \frac{h}{\ln \left(\frac{R + h/2}{R - h/2}\right)} = \frac{40}{\ln(140/60)} = 76.6 \, mm
\]

2ï¸âƒ£ \( e = R - r = 23.4 \, mm \)

3ï¸âƒ£ \( A = 60 Ã— 40 = 2400 \, mmÂ² \)

4ï¸âƒ£ Stress at inner fiber \( y = -20 \):
\[
Ïƒ_2 = \frac{M}{A e R} \left(1 + \frac{R e}{R - 20}\right)
\]
Substitute â†’ \( Ïƒ_2 = 75.8 \, MPa \)

5ï¸âƒ£ Stress at outer fiber \( y = +20 \):
\[
Ïƒ_1 = 53.2 \, MPa
\]

Hence,
- **Compressive stress (inner)** = 75.8 MPa  
- **Tensile stress (outer)** = 53.2 MPa  

---

## **Curved Ring under Uniform Load**

For circular rings (like chain links):
\[
M = \frac{W R}{2}
\]

Stress distribution depends on the curvature and the load angle.
Rings are designed for **equal strength** in both tension and compression sides.

---

## **Design Recommendations**

1. Neutral axis location must be accurately determined.  
2. Always check both tensile and compressive limits.  
3. Provide generous radii of curvature to reduce stress concentration.  
4. Use fillets at junctions (for crane hooks).  
5. Verify experimentally for forged or cast components.

---

## **Key Formulas Summary**

| Quantity | Expression | Notes |
|-----------|-------------|-------|
| Neutral axis radius | \( r = \frac{h}{\ln \frac{R + h/2}{R - h/2}} \) | Rectangular section |
| Eccentricity | \( e = R - r \) | Shift of N.A. |
| Bending stress | \( Ïƒ = \frac{M}{A e R} (1 + \frac{R e}{R + y}) \) | General form |
| Outer stress | \( Ïƒ_1 = \frac{M(R + h/2)}{A e (R - e)} \) | Tensile |
| Inner stress | \( Ïƒ_2 = \frac{M(R - h/2)}{A e (R - e)} \) | Compressive |

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- Curved beams experience **non-linear stress** distribution.  
- Neutral axis moves **inward** toward the center of curvature.  
- Inner fiber stress is **greater** than outer fiber stress.  
- Winklerâ€“Bach theory gives accurate stress prediction.  
- Design must ensure both safety and efficiency for bending loads.

---

> â€œIn curved forms, strength hides in geometry.  
> Understanding curvature is understanding balance.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---

á€™á€¼á€”á€ºá€™á€¬ + English bilingual Markdown (.md) format á€”á€²á€· á€¡á€á€±á€¸á€…á€­á€á€ºáŠ professional style á€–á€¼á€„á€·á€º á€›á€±á€¸á€•á€¼á€•á€±á€¸á€™á€šá€ºá‹

mechanical energy systems á€™á€»á€¬á€¸á€¡á€á€½á€€á€º á€¡á€á€¼á€±á€á€¶ á€á€˜á€±á€¬á€á€›á€¬á€¸á€á€½á€± á€•á€«á€á€„á€ºá€•á€«á€á€šá€ºá‹

# ğŸŒ€ Chapter 11 â€“ Springs (Helical, Leaf, and Spiral Springs)
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*

---

## **Introduction**

A **spring** is a mechanical element designed to **absorb, store, and release energy** when loaded.  
It deforms elastically and returns to its original shape after the load is removed.

Springs are widely used in:
- Vehicle suspension systems  
- Measuring instruments  
- Clutches and brakes  
- Energy storing devices  
- Safety valves and shock absorbers  

---

## **Classification of Springs**

| Type | Description | Common Use |
|------|--------------|-------------|
| **Helical Springs** | Made from a wire coiled in the form of a helix | Shock absorbers, valves |
| **Leaf Springs** | Made of flat plates or leaves stacked together | Vehicles, trucks |
| **Spiral Springs** | Flat strip wound in spiral form | Watches, measuring reels |
| **Torsion Springs** | Stores energy by twisting | Door hinges, toys |

---

## **Types of Helical Springs**

| Type | Shape | Load Direction |
|------|--------|----------------|
| **Close-coiled spring** | Coils nearly touching | Axial tension/compression |
| **Open-coiled spring** | Coils with large pitch angle | Axial or bending loads |
| **Conical / Volute spring** | Tapered shape | Space saving, variable stiffness |

---

## **Material Used**

Common spring materials must have **high elastic limit**, **fatigue strength**, and **resilience**.

| Material | Property |
|-----------|-----------|
| High-carbon steel (music wire) | General purpose |
| Stainless steel | Corrosion resistance |
| Phosphor bronze | Electrical applications |
| Silicon manganese steel | Heavy-duty springs |
| Beryllium copper | Precision instruments |

---

## **Stress in Helical Springs**

When an axial load **W** is applied, each coil of the spring is subjected to **torsional shear stress**.

Let  
- \( W \) = axial load  
- \( R \) = mean radius of coil  
- \( d \) = wire diameter  
- \( n \) = number of active coils  
- \( C = \frac{D}{d} \) = spring index  
- \( Ï„ \) = maximum shear stress  

Then,
\[
Ï„ = \frac{16 W R}{Ï€ d^3} \times K
\]
where  
\[
K = \frac{4C - 1}{4C - 4} + \frac{0.615}{C}
\]
is **Wahlâ€™s stress correction factor**, accounting for curvature effects.

---

## **Deflection of Helical Spring**

The deflection (elongation or compression) under load **W** is:

\[
Î´ = \frac{8 W R^3 n}{G d^4}
\]

where  
\( G \) = Modulus of rigidity of the spring material.

---

## **Stiffness of Spring**

\[
k = \frac{W}{Î´} = \frac{G d^4}{8 R^3 n}
\]

- High **k** = stiff spring  
- Low **k** = soft spring  

---

## **Strain Energy Stored**

The energy stored in a spring under load **W** is:

\[
U = \frac{1}{2} W Î´ = \frac{1}{2} k Î´^2
\]

This is the **elastic potential energy**, recoverable when load is removed.

---

## **Design Parameters**

To design a helical spring safely:

1. **Select material** based on application.  
2. **Choose spring index (C)** between 4 and 12.  
3. **Determine wire diameter (d)** using allowable shear stress.  
4. **Compute mean radius (R)** and number of coils (n).  
5. **Check for deflection** and **stiffness** limits.  
6. **Provide factor of safety (FOS)** â‰ˆ 1.5â€“2.5.

---

## **Example Calculation**

Given:  
W = 500 N, R = 40 mm, d = 8 mm, n = 10, G = 80 GPa  

Find: deflection and stress.

\[
Ï„ = \frac{16 Ã— 500 Ã— 40}{Ï€ Ã— 8^3} Ã— 1.15 = 76.3 \, MPa
\]
\[
Î´ = \frac{8 Ã— 500 Ã— 40^3 Ã— 10}{80Ã—10^3 Ã— 8^4} = 17.5 \, mm
\]

Hence,  
- **Maximum stress = 76.3 MPa**  
- **Deflection = 17.5 mm**

---

## **Open-Coiled Helical Springs**

When the coils are open with pitch angle **Î±**,  
the spring is subjected to both **bending** and **torsion**.

Deflection:
\[
Î´ = \frac{64 W R^3 n (1 + \frac{3}{2} \tan^2 Î±)}{G d^4}
\]

---

## **Leaf Springs**

A **leaf spring** consists of several **flat plates (leaves)**  
of varying length clamped together to act as one unit.  
They are used in **automobile suspensions** to absorb shocks.

---

### **Types**

- **Semi-elliptic (common type)**  
- **Quarter-elliptic**  
- **Three-quarter elliptic**  
- **Cantilever leaf spring**

---

### **Stress in Leaf Spring**

\[
Ïƒ = \frac{3 W L}{2 n b t^2}
\]
where  
\( L \) = half-length of spring,  
\( b \) = width of leaf,  
\( t \) = thickness of each leaf,  
\( n \) = number of leaves.

---

### **Deflection**

\[
Î´ = \frac{3 W L^3}{8 n b t^3 E}
\]

---

### **Energy Stored**

\[
U = \frac{1}{2} W Î´
\]

Leaf springs are designed for both **comfort and strength**,  
balancing stiffness and flexibility.

---

## **Spiral Springs**

A **spiral spring** consists of a flat strip wound in a spiral form.  
Used in **clocks**, **measuring tapes**, and **balance instruments**.

Torque:
\[
T = E b t^3 Î¸ / (12 L)
\]
Energy stored:
\[
U = \frac{1}{2} T Î¸
\]

where  
\( b \) = width, \( t \) = thickness, \( L \) = length of strip, \( Î¸ \) = angular twist.

---

## **Combination of Springs**

### **Springs in Series**
\[
\frac{1}{k_{eq}} = \frac{1}{k_1} + \frac{1}{k_2} + ...
\]
â†’ Greater deflection, lower stiffness.

### **Springs in Parallel**
\[
k_{eq} = k_1 + k_2 + ...
\]
â†’ Lower deflection, higher stiffness.

---

## **Failure Modes of Springs**

1. **Fatigue failure** (due to cyclic loading)  
2. **Buckling** (in compression)  
3. **Corrosion or pitting**  
4. **Overstressing beyond yield point**

---

## **Design Considerations**

- Avoid sharp corners â†’ use polished fillets.  
- Provide shot-peening for fatigue resistance.  
- Ensure proper heat treatment and pre-setting.  
- Allow for space and end conditions (plain, squared, ground).  

---

## **Key Formulas Summary**

| Quantity | Formula | Notes |
|-----------|----------|-------|
| Shear stress | \( Ï„ = \frac{16 W R K}{Ï€ d^3} \) | Close-coiled |
| Deflection | \( Î´ = \frac{8 W R^3 n}{G d^4} \) | Linear deflection |
| Stiffness | \( k = \frac{G d^4}{8 R^3 n} \) | |
| Energy stored | \( U = \frac{1}{2} W Î´ \) | Elastic energy |
| Leaf spring stress | \( Ïƒ = \frac{3 W L}{2 n b t^2} \) | |
| Leaf spring deflection | \( Î´ = \frac{3 W L^3}{8 n b t^3 E} \) | |
| Spiral spring torque | \( T = \frac{E b t^3 Î¸}{12 L} \) | Angular deflection |

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- Springs are essential energy-storing components in mechanics.  
- Design requires careful balance of stiffness, deflection, and safety.  
- Close-coiled helical springs use torsion; leaf springs use bending.  
- Fatigue and corrosion are the main causes of spring failure.  
- Efficiency increases with good material, proper geometry, and treatment.

---

> â€œSprings may look simple â€” yet they embody the purest form of stored potential.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---

(Eulerâ€™s theory, Rankineâ€™s formula, end conditions, effective length) á€€á€­á€¯ á€’á€®á€•á€¯á€¶á€…á€¶á€”á€²á€· á€†á€€á€ºá€›á€±á€¸á€•á€±á€¸á€™á€œá€¬á€¸?

á€™á€¼á€”á€ºá€™á€¬ + English bilingual Markdown (.md) format á€”á€²á€· á€¡á€á€±á€¸á€…á€­á€á€º professional style á€–á€¼á€„á€·á€º á€›á€±á€¸á€•á€¼á€•á€±á€¸á€™á€šá€ºá‹

á€’á€®á€¡á€á€”á€ºá€¸á€€á€á€±á€¬á€· Compression Members (columns, struts, posts, frames) á€á€½á€±á€Ÿá€¬
á€˜á€šá€ºá€œá€­á€¯ buckling (á€œá€¾á€Šá€·á€ºá€€á€»á€á€¼á€„á€ºá€¸) á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€”á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€á€šá€ºá€†á€­á€¯á€á€¬ á€á€˜á€±á€¬á€á€›á€¬á€¸á€¡á€•á€±á€«á€º á€¡á€á€¼á€±á€á€¶á€‘á€¬á€¸á€á€²á€·
engineering design theory á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€šá€ºá‹

# ğŸ—ï¸ Chapter 12 â€“ Columns and Struts (Buckling and Stability)
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*

---

## **Introduction**

A **column** (or strut) is a member that carries **axial compressive load**.  
If the member is long and slender, it may fail by **buckling** rather than crushing.  

- **Short column** â†’ fails by *direct compression (crushing)*.  
- **Long column** â†’ fails by *buckling (elastic instability)*.  

> Buckling means the sudden lateral bending of a slender member under compressive load.

---

## **Definitions**

| Term | Meaning |
|------|----------|
| **Column** | Vertical compression member (supports roofs, floors, etc.) |
| **Strut** | Compression member in any orientation (not necessarily vertical) |
| **Buckling load** | Load at which column just begins to buckle |
| **Crippling load (Pcr)** | Maximum safe load before instability |
| **Effective length (Le)** | Equivalent length of column with given end conditions |

---

## **Types of End Conditions**

| Condition | Description | Effective Length (Le) |
|------------|--------------|------------------------|
| Both ends hinged | Free to rotate | \( L_e = L \) |
| Both ends fixed | Fully restrained | \( L_e = 0.5L \) |
| One end fixed, other free | Cantilever | \( L_e = 2L \) |
| One end fixed, other hinged | Partially restrained | \( L_e = 0.7L \) |

---

## **Eulerâ€™s Column Theory (Elastic Buckling)**

**Assumptions:**
1. Column is perfectly straight, homogeneous, and elastic.  
2. Load is applied axially and centrally.  
3. Material follows Hookeâ€™s law.  
4. No initial imperfections.  

---

### **Eulerâ€™s Critical Load Formula**

\[
P_{cr} = \frac{Ï€^2 E I}{(L_e)^2}
\]

where  
- \( P_{cr} \) = Critical buckling load  
- \( E \) = Youngâ€™s modulus  
- \( I \) = Least moment of inertia of section  
- \( L_e \) = Effective length of column  

---

### **Euler Stress (Critical Stress)**

\[
Ïƒ_{cr} = \frac{P_{cr}}{A} = \frac{Ï€^2 E}{(L_e / r)^2}
\]

where  
\( r = \sqrt{I/A} \) = **radius of gyration**  

> The smaller the slenderness ratio \((L_e/r)\), the stronger the column.  

---

## **Slenderness Ratio**

\[
\text{Slenderness Ratio} = \frac{L_e}{r}
\]

| Type of Column | Typical Ratio | Mode of Failure |
|-----------------|----------------|----------------|
| Short column | < 40 | Crushing |
| Intermediate | 40â€“120 | Mixed |
| Long column | > 120 | Buckling |

---

## **Eulerâ€™s Curves and Limitations**

Eulerâ€™s formula is valid only for **long, slender columns** where buckling occurs before yield.  
It does **not apply** to:
- Short columns (material yielding dominates)  
- Columns with eccentric loading  
- Columns with initial curvature or defects  

---

## **Rankineâ€“Gordon Formula (Empirical Combination)**

To combine both **crushing** and **buckling** effects,  
Rankine proposed the empirical formula:

\[
\frac{1}{P_{cr}} = \frac{1}{P_c} + \frac{1}{P_e}
\]

or

\[
P_{cr} = \frac{Ïƒ_c A}{1 + Î± \left(\frac{L_e}{r}\right)^2}
\]

where  
- \( Ïƒ_c \) = crushing strength (short column)  
- \( Î± \) = Rankine constant (depends on material)

| Material | Î± (approx.) |
|-----------|-------------|
| Mild Steel | 1/7500 |
| Wrought Iron | 1/9000 |
| Cast Iron | 1/1600 |
| Timber | 1/4000 |

---

## **Comparison of Euler and Rankine**

| Aspect | Euler | Rankine |
|---------|--------|----------|
| Basis | Theoretical | Empirical |
| Valid for | Long columns | All lengths |
| Load type | Elastic buckling | Crushing + buckling |
| Accuracy | High (for slender) | Moderate (for all) |

---

## **Effective Lengths (Detailed)**

| End Condition | Diagram | Le |
|----------------|----------|----|
| Fixedâ€“Fixed | [||] | L/2 |
| Fixedâ€“Hinged | [|â€“O] | 0.7L |
| Hingedâ€“Hinged | [Oâ€“O] | L |
| Fixedâ€“Free | [|â€“] | 2L |

> **Note:** Effective length directly affects critical load \( P_{cr} \propto 1/L_e^2 \)

---

## **Example Calculation**

Given:  
Steel column, \( L = 2.5 m, D = 50 mm, E = 200 GPa, \) both ends hinged.  

\[
I = \frac{Ï€ D^4}{64} = \frac{Ï€(0.05)^4}{64} = 3.07 Ã— 10^{-8} m^4
\]
\[
P_{cr} = \frac{Ï€^2 Ã— 200Ã—10^9 Ã— 3.07Ã—10^{-8}}{(2.5)^2} = 9.7 \, kN
\]

Thus, **critical load = 9.7 kN**.

---

## **Eccentric Loading on Columns**

If load acts at an eccentricity **e**, additional **bending stress** occurs.

\[
Ïƒ_{max} = \frac{P}{A} + \frac{M y}{I}
\]

where  
\( M = P e \), and \( y = \) distance from neutral axis.

> Columns under eccentric loads always fail at lower loads than theoretical Euler loads.

---

## **Johnsonâ€™s Parabolic Formula**

For **intermediate columns**,  
neither pure crushing nor pure buckling dominates.  

Johnson proposed:
\[
Ïƒ = Ïƒ_c \left[1 - \frac{1}{2} \left(\frac{L_e}{r} \frac{Ïƒ_c}{Ï€^2 E}\right)^2\right]
\]

Valid for \( \frac{L_e}{r} < 120 \)

---

## **Struts and Built-up Columns**

In large structures (bridges, cranes, towers),  
**built-up columns** are used (I-sections, latticed, tubular).  
They improve **buckling resistance** and reduce **weight**.

- **Lacing** â†’ diagonal bars prevent buckling.  
- **Batting** â†’ solid plates connect members.  

---

## **Experimentally Determining Buckling**

- Apply axial load slowly.  
- Measure lateral deflection.  
- Record load at which deflection increases suddenly â†’ **critical load**.  
- Plot load vs. deflection curve â†’ stability limit.

---

## **Design Guidelines**

1. Always compute effective length according to end restraint.  
2. Ensure eccentricity < allowable value.  
3. Select section with **high radius of gyration (r)**.  
4. Apply proper factor of safety (2.5â€“3.0).  
5. Use Rankineâ€™s formula for practical design.  

---

## **Key Formulas Summary**

| Quantity | Formula | Remarks |
|-----------|----------|----------|
| Eulerâ€™s Load | \( P_{cr} = \frac{Ï€^2 E I}{(L_e)^2} \) | Long columns |
| Euler Stress | \( Ïƒ_{cr} = \frac{Ï€^2 E}{(L_e/r)^2} \) | |
| Rankineâ€™s Load | \( P = \frac{Ïƒ_c A}{1 + Î±(L_e/r)^2} \) | All columns |
| Slenderness Ratio | \( \frac{L_e}{r} \) | |
| Radius of Gyration | \( r = \sqrt{I/A} \) | |
| Johnsonâ€™s Formula | \( Ïƒ = Ïƒ_c [1 - \frac{1}{2} (\frac{Ïƒ_c (L_e/r)}{Ï€^2 E})^2] \) | Intermediate columns |

---

## **Practical Applications**

| Application | Example |
|--------------|----------|
| Building frames | Steel columns, reinforced concrete pillars |
| Machines | Lathe tailstock, piston rods |
| Vehicles | Struts in suspension systems |
| Bridges & Towers | Lattice or tubular compression members |

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- **Buckling** is a form of *instability*, not material failure.  
- **Eulerâ€™s theory** is valid for long slender columns only.  
- **Rankineâ€™s formula** gives reliable results for all cases.  
- **Effective length** and **end condition** greatly influence stability.  
- Always check both **strength** and **stiffness** for safe design.

---

> â€œA column never fails suddenly â€” it warns through instability.  
> Stability, not strength, defines safety.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---
