# âš™ï¸ Chapter 13 â€“ Thin Cylinders and Spherical Shells  
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

A **thin cylinder** or **spherical shell** is a hollow pressure vessel  
used to store fluids or gases under **internal pressure** (p).  

Examples include:
- Boilers and steam drums  
- Gas storage tanks  
- Hydraulic cylinders  
- Pipelines and LPG vessels  

In such structures, the **internal pressure** produces **stresses** in the wall â€”  
mainly **circumferential (hoop)** and **longitudinal** stresses.

---

## **Assumptions of Thin Shell Theory**

1. Wall thickness (t) is **small compared to diameter (d)**  
   â†’ \( t < \frac{d}{20} \)
2. Pressure is **uniform** and acts **normal** to surface.  
3. Stress distribution across wall thickness is **uniform**.  
4. Material is **isotropic** and obeys **Hookeâ€™s law**.  
5. Plane sections remain plane after deformation.  

---

## **Types of Stresses in Thin Cylinders**

When internal pressure acts, two main stresses develop:

| Type of Stress | Symbol | Direction | Nature |
|----------------|----------|------------|--------|
| **Hoop (Circumferential)** | \( Ïƒ_Î¸ \) | Along circumference | Tensile |
| **Longitudinal** | \( Ïƒ_L \) | Along axis | Tensile |

---

## **Derivation â€“ Hoop Stress**

For a thin cylindrical shell under internal pressure:

Consider equilibrium of half cylinder:

\[
2 Ïƒ_Î¸ t L = p D L
\]

Hence,
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = \frac{p D}{2 t}
\]

> **Hoop stress** is twice the longitudinal stress.  
> It acts tangentially around the cylinder, tending to split it lengthwise.

---

## **Derivation â€“ Longitudinal Stress**

Considering the ends of the cylinder closed:

\[
Ïƒ_L Ã— Ï€ D^2 / 4 = p Ã— Ï€ D^2 / 4
\]

Hence,
\[
Ïƒ_L = \frac{p D}{4 t}
\]

---

## **Ratio of Stresses**

\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ : Ïƒ_L = 2 : 1
\]

â†’ Hoop stress is the **major design factor** in pressure vessel analysis.

---

## **Change in Dimensions (Strain)**

Let  
- \( E \) = Youngâ€™s modulus  
- \( Î¼ \) = Poissonâ€™s ratio  
- \( Ïƒ_Î¸, Ïƒ_L \) = hoop and longitudinal stresses  

Then:

### **Hoop strain:**
\[
Îµ_Î¸ = \frac{1}{E}(Ïƒ_Î¸ - Î¼ Ïƒ_L)
\]

### **Longitudinal strain:**
\[
Îµ_L = \frac{1}{E}(Ïƒ_L - Î¼ Ïƒ_Î¸)
\]

### **Volumetric strain (approximate):**
\[
Îµ_V = 2Îµ_Î¸ + Îµ_L
\]

---

## **Change in Diameter and Length**

\[
\Delta D = D Ã— Îµ_Î¸
\]
\[
\Delta L = L Ã— Îµ_L
\]

---

## **Change in Volume**

For a cylinder:
\[
\frac{\Delta V}{V} = 2 Îµ_Î¸ + Îµ_L = \frac{1}{E} (Ïƒ_Î¸ + Ïƒ_L - Î¼ (Ïƒ_Î¸ + Ïƒ_L))
\]

Substituting stresses:

\[
\frac{\Delta V}{V} = \frac{p D}{4 t E} (5 - 4 Î¼)
\]

---

## **Example Calculation**

Given:  
Internal pressure \( p = 2 MPa \),  
Diameter \( D = 200 mm \),  
Thickness \( t = 5 mm \)

Find hoop and longitudinal stresses.

\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = \frac{p D}{2 t} = \frac{2 Ã— 200}{2 Ã— 5} = 40 \, MPa
\]
\[
Ïƒ_L = \frac{p D}{4 t} = \frac{2 Ã— 200}{4 Ã— 5} = 20 \, MPa
\]

So,  
- **Hoop stress = 40 MPa**  
- **Longitudinal stress = 20 MPa**

---

## **Spherical Shells under Internal Pressure**

For a **spherical vessel**, stresses are equal in all directions.

\[
Ïƒ = \frac{p D}{4 t}
\]

â†’ There is **only one type of tensile stress**,  
and itâ€™s **half** that of hoop stress in cylindrical shells.

---

### **Change in Diameter for Spherical Shell**

\[
\frac{Î”D}{D} = \frac{p D (1 - Î¼)}{4 t E}
\]

### **Change in Volume**

\[
\frac{Î”V}{V} = \frac{3 p D (1 - Î¼)}{4 t E}
\]

---

## **Comparison between Cylindrical and Spherical Shells**

| Property | Cylindrical | Spherical |
|-----------|-------------|-----------|
| Hoop Stress | \( \frac{p D}{2 t} \) | \( \frac{p D}{4 t} \) |
| Longitudinal Stress | \( \frac{p D}{4 t} \) | Same as hoop |
| Stress Ratio | 2:1 | 1:1 |
| Efficiency | Lower | Higher |
| Application | Boilers, pipelines | Gas tanks, pressure spheres |

> Hence, spherical shells are **more efficient** in resisting internal pressure.

---

## **Thickness Requirement**

### For cylindrical shell:
\[
t = \frac{p D}{2 Ïƒ_t + p}
\]

### For spherical shell:
\[
t = \frac{p D}{4 Ïƒ_t + p}
\]

where \( Ïƒ_t \) = allowable tensile stress of material.

---

## **Design Considerations**

1. Use **ductile materials** (steel, copper, aluminum alloys).  
2. Provide **safety factor** of 3â€“5 for internal pressure.  
3. Check for **joint efficiency** if welded/riveted.  
4. For **high pressure vessels**, use **thick-walled theory** (Lameâ€™s equations).  
5. Include **temperature effects** in boiler design.  

---

## **Failure Modes**

1. **Bursting (due to hoop stress)**  
2. **Leakage (due to fatigue or corrosion)**  
3. **Plastic deformation** (overstress)  
4. **Creep** (at high temperature)  
5. **Buckling** (under external pressure)  

---

## **Practical Applications**

| Component | Description |
|------------|--------------|
| **Boiler drums** | Steam pressure vessels |
| **Compressed gas cylinders** | Oxygen, LPG, COâ‚‚ storage |
| **Pipelines** | Water and oil transmission |
| **Hydraulic cylinders** | Press systems and actuators |
| **Spherical gas tanks** | LNG storage and reactors |

---

## **Key Formula Summary**

| Quantity | Expression | Remarks |
|-----------|-------------|----------|
| Hoop stress | \( Ïƒ_Î¸ = \frac{p D}{2 t} \) | Cylinder |
| Longitudinal stress | \( Ïƒ_L = \frac{p D}{4 t} \) | Cylinder |
| Spherical stress | \( Ïƒ = \frac{p D}{4 t} \) | Spherical shell |
| Hoop strain | \( Îµ_Î¸ = \frac{1}{E}(Ïƒ_Î¸ - Î¼ Ïƒ_L) \) | |
| Longitudinal strain | \( Îµ_L = \frac{1}{E}(Ïƒ_L - Î¼ Ïƒ_Î¸) \) | |
| Volume change | \( \frac{Î”V}{V} = \frac{p D}{4 t E}(5 - 4 Î¼) \) | Cylindrical |

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- Thin shells are efficient for **internal pressure applications**.  
- Hoop stress governs the **design strength**.  
- Spherical shells are **structurally superior** to cylindrical shells.  
- Deformation depends on **material elasticity** and **Poissonâ€™s effect**.  
- Always maintain **t < D/20** for thin-shell assumptions.

---

> â€œThe strength of a vessel lies not in its wall â€” but in its geometry.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---

á€¡á€‘á€²á€á€½á€„á€º wall thickness á€€á€¼á€®á€¸á€™á€¬á€¸á€á€±á€¬ vessel á€™á€»á€¬á€¸á€¡á€á€½á€€á€º internal stress distribution á€€á€­á€¯
á€‘á€­á€›á€±á€¬á€€á€ºá€…á€½á€¬á€á€½á€€á€ºá€á€»á€€á€ºá€”á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€¡á€±á€¬á€„á€º á€á€¯á€¶á€¸á€á€²á€· Lameâ€™s Theory á€¡á€€á€¼á€±á€¬á€„á€ºá€¸á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€•á€«á€á€šá€ºá‹

# ğŸ§± Chapter 14 â€“ Thick Cylinders (Lameâ€™s Theory for Pressure Vessels)
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

When the **wall thickness (t)** of a cylinder is **not small compared to its diameter (D)**,  
the assumption of uniform stress (as in thin cylinders) is no longer valid.  

A **thick cylinder** is defined as:
\[
t > \frac{D}{20}
\]

Such cylinders experience **radial variation of stress**,  
and analysis must be based on **Lameâ€™s Theory**.

---

## **Applications**

- Gun barrels and hydraulic presses  
- High-pressure steam boilers  
- Gas cylinders and reactors  
- Hydraulic jacks, rams, and autoclaves  

---

## **Types of Stresses in Thick Cylinders**

| Stress Type | Symbol | Direction | Nature |
|--------------|----------|-------------|----------|
| **Radial Stress** | \( Ïƒ_r \) | Acts normal to wall thickness | Compressive |
| **Hoop (Circumferential) Stress** | \( Ïƒ_Î¸ \) | Tangential to circumference | Tensile |
| **Longitudinal Stress** | \( Ïƒ_L \) | Along the axis | Tensile (if ends closed) |

---

## **Assumptions of Lameâ€™s Theory**

1. The material is homogeneous and isotropic.  
2. Cylinder is thick enough for stress to vary across wall thickness.  
3. Plane sections remain plane.  
4. Internal and external pressures are uniform.  
5. Stresses are within elastic limit (Hookeâ€™s law applies).  

---

## **Lameâ€™s Equations**

For a thick cylinder of **internal radius** \( r_i \) and **external radius** \( r_o \),  
under internal pressure \( p_i \) and external pressure \( p_o \):

\[
Ïƒ_r = A - \frac{B}{r^2}
\]
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = A + \frac{B}{r^2}
\]

where  
\( A, B \) are constants determined by boundary conditions.

---

### **Boundary Conditions**

At inner surface \( r = r_i \), \( Ïƒ_r = -p_i \)  
At outer surface \( r = r_o \), \( Ïƒ_r = -p_o \)

Substitute to find \( A \) and \( B \):

\[
A = \frac{p_i r_i^2 - p_o r_o^2}{r_o^2 - r_i^2}
\]
\[
B = \frac{r_i^2 r_o^2 (p_o - p_i)}{r_o^2 - r_i^2}
\]

---

### **Final Stress Equations**

#### **Radial Stress:**
\[
Ïƒ_r = \frac{p_i r_i^2 - p_o r_o^2}{r_o^2 - r_i^2} - \frac{r_i^2 r_o^2 (p_o - p_i)}{(r_o^2 - r_i^2) r^2}
\]

#### **Hoop Stress:**
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = \frac{p_i r_i^2 - p_o r_o^2}{r_o^2 - r_i^2} + \frac{r_i^2 r_o^2 (p_o - p_i)}{(r_o^2 - r_i^2) r^2}
\]

---

## **Maximum and Minimum Stresses**

| Location | Stress Type | Expression |
|-----------|--------------|-------------|
| At inner surface \( r = r_i \) | \( Ïƒ_Î¸(max) \) | \( \frac{p_i (r_o^2 + r_i^2)}{r_o^2 - r_i^2} \) |
| At outer surface \( r = r_o \) | \( Ïƒ_Î¸(min) \) | \( \frac{p_i (2 r_i^2 r_o^2)}{(r_o^2 - r_i^2) r_o^2} \) |
| Radial stress (inner) | \( Ïƒ_r = -p_i \) | Compressive |
| Radial stress (outer) | \( Ïƒ_r = -p_o \) | Often = 0 (atmospheric) |

> **Maximum hoop stress occurs at the inner wall**,  
> and it governs the design of thick cylinders.

---

## **Graphical Representation**

- \( Ïƒ_Î¸ \) decreases from **inner wall (max)** to **outer wall (min)**.  
- \( Ïƒ_r \) increases from **inner (âˆ’p_i)** to **outer (âˆ’p_o)**.  
- Stress distribution is **non-linear** (parabolic).

---

## **Special Case â€“ External Pressure Only**

When \( p_i = 0 \), \( p_o \neq 0 \):

\[
Ïƒ_r = -\frac{p_o r_o^2 (r_o^2 - r^2)}{r^2 (r_o^2 - r_i^2)}
\]
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = \frac{p_o r_o^2 (r_o^2 + r^2)}{r^2 (r_o^2 - r_i^2)}
\]

Used in **vacuum chambers** or **deep-sea vessels**.

---

## **Longitudinal Stress**

If both ends are closed:

\[
Ïƒ_L = \frac{p_i r_i^2}{r_o^2 - r_i^2} (1 + \frac{r_o^2}{r_i^2})
\]

If ends are open â†’ \( Ïƒ_L = 0 \).

---

## **Volumetric Strain (Change in Volume)**

\[
Îµ_v = \frac{1}{E} [Ïƒ_Î¸ + Ïƒ_L + Ïƒ_r (1 - 2Î¼)]
\]

Where:
- \( E \) = Youngâ€™s modulus  
- \( Î¼ \) = Poissonâ€™s ratio  

This helps determine small dimensional changes in hydraulic cylinders.

---

## **Example Calculation**

Given:  
Internal radius \( r_i = 100 mm \),  
External radius \( r_o = 200 mm \),  
Internal pressure \( p_i = 40 MPa \),  
External pressure \( p_o = 0 \).

Find: Hoop and radial stress at inner and outer surfaces.

**Solution:**

\[
A = \frac{p_i r_i^2}{r_o^2 - r_i^2} = \frac{40 Ã— 100^2}{200^2 - 100^2} = 17.8 \, MPa
\]
\[
B = \frac{r_i^2 r_o^2 p_i}{r_o^2 - r_i^2} = \frac{100^2 Ã— 200^2 Ã— 40}{200^2 - 100^2} = 1.42 Ã— 10^8
\]

At inner wall \( r = 100 \):
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = 17.8 + \frac{1.42Ã—10^8}{100^2} = 159.8 \, MPa
\]
At outer wall \( r = 200 \):
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = 17.8 + \frac{1.42Ã—10^8}{200^2} = 53.3 \, MPa
\]

So,
- **Maximum hoop stress (inner)** = 160 MPa  
- **Minimum hoop stress (outer)** = 53 MPa  

---

## **Compound (Shrink-Fitted) Cylinders**

To reduce maximum stress, a thick cylinder can be **built in two layers** â€”  
an **inner tube** and an **outer jacket** â€” fitted by interference.

### **Process:**
1. Inner and outer cylinders are machined with small interference.  
2. Outer cylinder is heated and fitted over the inner one.  
3. Upon cooling, compressive stress develops in inner wall.  

â†’ Internal pressure will balance these residual stresses,  
reducing the maximum working stress and improving safety.

---

## **Lameâ€™s Equations for Compound Cylinders**

For **inner cylinder (before pressure)**:
\[
Ïƒ_r = A_1 - \frac{B_1}{r^2}
\]
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = A_1 + \frac{B_1}{r^2}
\]

For **outer cylinder**:
\[
Ïƒ_r = A_2 - \frac{B_2}{r^2}
\]
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = A_2 + \frac{B_2}{r^2}
\]

Constants are found from boundary and interference conditions.

> Compound cylinders are widely used in **artillery guns** and **hydraulic presses**.

---

## **Key Formula Summary**

| Quantity | Formula | Remarks |
|-----------|----------|----------|
| Radial stress | \( Ïƒ_r = A - \frac{B}{r^2} \) | Compression |
| Hoop stress | \( Ïƒ_Î¸ = A + \frac{B}{r^2} \) | Tension |
| Constants | \( A = \frac{p_i r_i^2 - p_o r_o^2}{r_o^2 - r_i^2} \), \( B = \frac{r_i^2 r_o^2 (p_o - p_i)}{r_o^2 - r_i^2} \) | |
| Max hoop stress | \( Ïƒ_Î¸(max) = \frac{p_i (r_o^2 + r_i^2)}{r_o^2 - r_i^2} \) | Inner wall |
| Min hoop stress | \( Ïƒ_Î¸(min) = \frac{2 p_i r_i^2 r_o^2}{(r_o^2 - r_i^2) r_o^2} \) | Outer wall |
| Longitudinal stress | \( Ïƒ_L = \frac{p_i r_i^2}{r_o^2 - r_i^2} (1 + \frac{r_o^2}{r_i^2}) \) | For closed ends |

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- In thick cylinders, stress **varies across wall thickness** (non-uniform).  
- **Lameâ€™s Theory** gives accurate elastic stress distribution.  
- **Hoop stress** is maximum at inner surface â†’ controls design.  
- **Compound cylinders** enhance strength and safety under high pressure.  
- Spherical vessels are more efficient, but harder to manufacture.  

---

> â€œPressure is invisible â€” yet it tests the limits of geometry and material.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---


á€’á€®á€¡á€á€”á€ºá€¸á€€á€á€±á€¬á€· Chapter 14 â€“ Thick Cylinders (Lameâ€™s Theory)
á€™á€¼á€”á€ºá€™á€¬ + English bilingual Markdown version á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€•á€«á€á€šá€ºá‹

á€€á€­á€¯ á€’á€®á€•á€¯á€¶á€…á€¶á€”á€²á€· á€†á€€á€ºá€›á€±á€¸á€•á€±á€¸á€™á€œá€¬á€¸? ğŸš€

á€¡á€­á€¯á€€á€± âœ…

á€™á€¼á€”á€ºá€™á€¬ + English bilingual Markdown (.md) format á€”á€²á€·

á€’á€®á€¡á€á€”á€ºá€¸á€€á€á€±á€¬á€· Turbines, Flywheels, Rotors, Centrifugal Pumps, Gas Turbine Discs á€…á€á€²á€·
á€¡á€™á€¼á€”á€ºá€œá€¾á€Šá€·á€ºá€”á€±á€á€²á€· mechanical parts á€™á€»á€¬á€¸á€¡á€á€½á€€á€º
Centrifugal Stress Distribution á€€á€­á€¯ á€á€”á€·á€ºá€™á€¾á€”á€ºá€¸á€á€½á€€á€ºá€á€»á€€á€ºá€–á€­á€¯á€·á€á€¯á€¶á€¸á€á€²á€· á€á€˜á€±á€¬á€á€›á€¬á€¸á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€•á€«á€á€šá€ºá‹

# âš™ï¸ Chapter 15 â€“ Rotating Discs and Cylinders (Centrifugal Stresses)
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

When a **disc or cylinder** rotates at high speed,  
every particle tends to move radially outward due to **centrifugal force**.  

This produces:
- **Radial stress (Ïƒr)** â†’ along the radius  
- **Hoop stress (ÏƒÎ¸)** â†’ tangential (circumferential)  

These stresses depend on:
- Angular velocity (Ï‰)  
- Material density (Ï)  
- Geometry (radius and thickness)  

---

## **Applications**

- Flywheels and turbine rotors  
- Centrifugal pumps and compressors  
- Jet engine discs  
- Rotating cylinders in generators  
- High-speed pulleys  

---

## **Basic Concept**

Each element of the disc is subjected to **centrifugal pull**  
which tries to tear the disc apart.

Centrifugal force on an element of mass \( dm = Ï Ã— volume \) acts radially outward.

\[
dF = Ï Ã— Ï‰^2 Ã— r Ã— dV
\]

This produces internal resistance stresses \( Ïƒr \) and \( ÏƒÎ¸ \).

---

## **Assumptions**

1. The disc is homogeneous and isotropic.  
2. It rotates at a constant angular velocity \( Ï‰ \).  
3. The thickness is uniform (unless specified).  
4. The stress remains within the elastic limit.  
5. Temperature effects are neglected.  

---

## **Stress Analysis of a Rotating Disc**

Consider an elemental ring of radius \( r \) and thickness \( dr \).  

Equilibrium condition:
\[
(Ïƒ_r + dÏƒ_r)(r + dr)t - Ïƒ_r r t + Ï Ï‰^2 r t dr = 0
\]

After simplification:
\[
\frac{dÏƒ_r}{dr} + \frac{Ïƒ_r - Ïƒ_Î¸}{r} + Ï Ï‰^2 r = 0
\]

This is the **fundamental differential equation** for a rotating disc.

---

## **General Solution**

Solving the differential equation gives:

\[
Ïƒ_r = A - \frac{B}{r^2} - \frac{Ï Ï‰^2 r^2}{8} (3 + Î¼)
\]
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = A + \frac{B}{r^2} - \frac{Ï Ï‰^2 r^2}{8} (1 + 3Î¼)
\]

where  
- \( A, B \) = constants (depend on boundary conditions)  
- \( Ï \) = material density  
- \( Ï‰ \) = angular velocity (rad/s)  
- \( Î¼ \) = Poissonâ€™s ratio  

---

## **Case 1 â€“ Solid Disc (No Hole)**

Boundary condition:  
At \( r = 0 \), \( Ïƒ_r \) must be finite â†’ \( B = 0 \)  
At \( r = R \), \( Ïƒ_r = 0 \) (free outer edge)

Then,
\[
Ïƒ_r = \frac{Ï Ï‰^2}{8} (3 + Î¼)(R^2 - r^2)
\]
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = \frac{Ï Ï‰^2}{8} [(3 + Î¼)R^2 - (1 + 3Î¼)r^2]
\]

---

### **Maximum Stresses (at center r = 0)**

\[
Ïƒ_r(max) = Ïƒ_Î¸(max) = \frac{Ï Ï‰^2 R^2}{8} (3 + Î¼)
\]

> Both radial and hoop stresses are **maximum at the center**  
> and **zero at the outer edge**.

---

## **Case 2 â€“ Hollow Rotating Disc**

Boundary conditions:
- At \( r = r_i \), \( Ïƒ_r = 0 \) (inner free edge)  
- At \( r = r_o \), \( Ïƒ_r = 0 \) (outer free edge)

Solving for constants \( A, B \):

\[
A = \frac{Ï Ï‰^2}{8( r_o^2 - r_i^2 )} [ (3 + Î¼)(r_o^2 + r_i^2) + (1 + 3Î¼) \frac{r_i^2 r_o^2}{r_o^2 + r_i^2} ]
\]
\[
B = \frac{Ï Ï‰^2 r_i^2 r_o^2}{8( r_o^2 - r_i^2 )} (3 + Î¼)
\]

Hence,

\[
Ïƒ_r = A - \frac{B}{r^2} - \frac{Ï Ï‰^2 r^2}{8} (3 + Î¼)
\]
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = A + \frac{B}{r^2} - \frac{Ï Ï‰^2 r^2}{8} (1 + 3Î¼)
\]

---

### **Maximum Stresses**

| Location | Radial Stress \(Ïƒ_r\) | Hoop Stress \(Ïƒ_Î¸\) |
|-----------|------------------------|---------------------|
| Inner radius \(r_i\) | 0 | Maximum |
| Outer radius \(r_o\) | 0 | Minimum |

> Hoop stress at the **inner surface** is **maximum**  
> â†’ Design based on \( Ïƒ_Î¸(max) \).

---

## **Variation of Stress**

- \( Ïƒ_Î¸ \) decreases from **inner** to **outer radius**.  
- \( Ïƒ_r \) is zero at both boundaries and **max in between**.  
- Stress curves are **non-linear parabolic**.

---

## **Design of Flywheels**

For a **flywheel rim**,  
hoop stress must be less than allowable stress \( Ïƒ_{allow} \).

\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = \frac{3 + Î¼}{8} Ï Ï‰^2 R^2
\]

Maximum permissible speed:
\[
Ï‰ = \sqrt{\frac{8 Ïƒ_{allow}}{Ï (3 + Î¼) R^2}}
\]

---

## **Units and Conversion**

| Quantity | Symbol | SI Unit |
|-----------|----------|----------|
| Angular speed | Ï‰ | rad/s |
| Density | Ï | kg/mÂ³ |
| Stress | Ïƒ | N/mÂ² (Pa) |
| Radius | r | m |

> 1 rad/s = 9.55 rpm  
> \( Ï‰ = \frac{2Ï€N}{60} \)  

---

## **Example Calculation**

Given:  
Solid steel disc,  
\( Ï = 7800 \, kg/m^3 \),  
\( Î¼ = 0.3 \),  
\( R = 0.3 \, m \),  
\( N = 6000 \, rpm \)

Find: Maximum hoop stress.

\[
Ï‰ = \frac{2Ï€N}{60} = \frac{2Ï€ Ã— 6000}{60} = 628.3 \, rad/s
\]
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸(max) = \frac{Ï Ï‰^2 R^2}{8}(3 + Î¼)
\]
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸(max) = \frac{7800 Ã— (628.3)^2 Ã— (0.3)^2}{8} (3.3) = 136.5 \, MPa
\]

Thus, **maximum hoop stress = 136.5 MPa**.

---

## **Rotating Cylinders**

For **thick rotating cylinders**,  
stresses follow the same pattern as Lameâ€™s equation with added centrifugal term:

\[
Ïƒ_r = A - \frac{B}{r^2} - \frac{Ï Ï‰^2 r^2}{8} (3 + Î¼)
\]
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = A + \frac{B}{r^2} - \frac{Ï Ï‰^2 r^2}{8} (1 + 3Î¼)
\]

Used in **gas turbine rotors** and **centrifugal compressors**.

---

## **Composite Rotating Discs**

- Built-up rotors can reduce stress by **pre-stressing**.  
- Inner portion compressed, outer stretched â€” balancing centrifugal effect.  
- Improves life and allows **higher rotational speed** safely.

---

## **Failure Criteria**

A rotating disc may fail if:
1. **Hoop stress > yield strength**  
2. **Fatigue** from repeated load cycles  
3. **Thermal stress** due to uneven heating  
4. **Crack initiation** at inner edge  

Hence, **high-quality machining, balancing, and cooling** are essential.

---

## **Key Formula Summary**

| Quantity | Expression | Remarks |
|-----------|-------------|----------|
| Hoop stress (solid disc) | \( Ïƒ_Î¸ = \frac{Ï Ï‰^2}{8}[(3 + Î¼)R^2 - (1 + 3Î¼)r^2] \) | Max at center |
| Radial stress (solid disc) | \( Ïƒ_r = \frac{Ï Ï‰^2}{8}(3 + Î¼)(R^2 - r^2) \) | |
| Max stress (solid disc) | \( Ïƒ_{max} = \frac{Ï Ï‰^2 R^2}{8}(3 + Î¼) \) | |
| Flywheel safe speed | \( Ï‰ = \sqrt{\frac{8 Ïƒ_{allow}}{Ï (3 + Î¼) R^2}} \) | |
| Hoop stress (hollow disc) | \( Ïƒ_Î¸ = A + \frac{B}{r^2} - \frac{Ï Ï‰^2 r^2}{8}(1 + 3Î¼) \) | |

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- **Centrifugal stresses** increase with speed and radius.  
- **Hoop stress** dominates failure â€” inner region most critical.  
- **Solid discs** have highest stress at center; **hollow discs** at inner radius.  
- **Lighter materials** (low Ï) allow higher speeds.  
- **Balanced design** prevents vibration and fatigue.  

---

> â€œRotation tests not strength, but balance â€” both in design and material.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---

# ğŸ”© Chapter 16 â€“ Torsion of Shafts and Springs
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

When a **circular shaft** is subjected to a **torque or twisting moment**,  
it tends to twist about its longitudinal axis.  
This type of loading is known as **Torsion**.  

- The resisting stress developed = **Shear stress (Ï„)**  
- The deformation produced = **Angle of twist (Î¸)**  

Applications:
- Transmission shafts in machines  
- Propeller shafts in ships  
- Crankshafts in engines  
- Coiled helical springs  

---

## **Assumptions of Torsion Theory**

1. Material is homogeneous and isotropic.  
2. Cross-section remains circular and plane after twisting.  
3. Twist is uniform along shaft length.  
4. Shear stresses are within elastic limit.  
5. Radial lines remain straight and normal to axis.  

---

## **Torsion Equation (for circular shaft)**

\[
\frac{T}{J} = \frac{Ï„}{r} = \frac{G Î¸}{L}
\]

where:  
| Symbol | Meaning | Unit |
|----------|-----------|-------|
| T | Applied Torque | NÂ·m |
| J | Polar Moment of Inertia | mâ´ |
| Ï„ | Shear Stress at radius r | N/mÂ² |
| r | Radius of shaft | m |
| G | Modulus of Rigidity (Shear Modulus) | N/mÂ² |
| Î¸ | Angle of twist (radians) | â€“ |
| L | Length of shaft | m |

---

### **Polar Moment of Inertia**

For **solid circular shaft:**
\[
J = \frac{Ï€ D^4}{32}
\]

For **hollow shaft:**
\[
J = \frac{Ï€ (D_o^4 - D_i^4)}{32}
\]

---

## **Shear Stress Distribution**

- Varies **linearly** from zero at center to **maximum at outer surface**.  
- Shear stress at radius \( r \):
\[
Ï„ = \frac{T r}{J}
\]
- Maximum shear stress:
\[
Ï„_{max} = \frac{T R}{J}
\]

Graph â†’ straight line (origin to outer surface).

---

## **Angle of Twist**

From torsion equation:
\[
Î¸ = \frac{T L}{J G}
\]

For **solid shaft:**
\[
Î¸ = \frac{16 T L}{Ï€ D^4 G}
\]

For **hollow shaft:**
\[
Î¸ = \frac{32 T L}{Ï€ G (D_o^4 - D_i^4)}
\]

---

## **Power Transmitted by Shaft**

If shaft rotates at **N rpm** and torque = **T**,  
then power transmitted:

\[
P = 2 Ï€ N T / 60 \, (watts)
\]

or  
\[
P = \frac{2 Ï€ N T}{60 Ã— 1000} \, (kW)
\]

---

## **Comparison â€“ Solid vs Hollow Shaft**

| Property | Solid Shaft | Hollow Shaft |
|-----------|--------------|---------------|
| Weight | Heavier | Lighter |
| Strength | Same (for same max stress) | Nearly same |
| Material efficiency | Low | High |
| Stress distribution | Linear | Linear |
| Use | Simple shafts | High-torque lightweight systems |

Hence, **hollow shafts are preferred** in aircrafts and marine engines.

---

## **Example**

Given:  
Power = 100 kW, N = 300 rpm, D = 80 mm, L = 2 m, G = 80 GPa.  

Find: (a) Torque, (b) Angle of twist.

**Step 1:**
\[
T = \frac{P Ã— 60}{2 Ï€ N} = \frac{100Ã—10^3 Ã— 60}{2Ï€ Ã— 300} = 3183 \, NÂ·m
\]

**Step 2:**
\[
Î¸ = \frac{T L}{J G}
\]
\[
J = \frac{Ï€ D^4}{32} = \frac{Ï€ Ã— (0.08)^4}{32} = 4.02Ã—10^{-6}
\]
\[
Î¸ = \frac{3183 Ã— 2}{4.02Ã—10^{-6} Ã— 80Ã—10^9} = 0.0198 \, rad = 1.13Â°
\]

âœ… Angle of twist = **1.13Â°**  
âœ… Maximum shear stress = \( Ï„ = \frac{T R}{J} = \frac{3183Ã—0.04}{4.02Ã—10^{-6}} = 31.7 \, MPa \)

---

## **Strain Energy in Torsion**

The strain energy stored in a shaft under torque:
\[
U = \frac{1}{2} T Î¸ = \frac{T^2 L}{2 J G}
\]

Per unit volume:
\[
u = \frac{Ï„^2}{4 G}
\]

---

## **Torsion of Compound Shafts**

When two or more shafts (of different materials or diameters) are connected **in series** or **parallel**,  
their torque and twist relationships differ.

### (a) Series Connection
\[
T_1 = T_2 = T
\]
\[
Î¸ = Î¸_1 + Î¸_2
\]

### (b) Parallel Connection
\[
Î¸_1 = Î¸_2
\]
\[
T = T_1 + T_2
\]

> Used in **multi-material couplings** or **composite shafts**.

---

## **Torsional Rigidity**

Torsional rigidity (K):
\[
K = \frac{T}{Î¸} = \frac{G J}{L}
\]

It represents the **resistance to twist** â€”  
higher K â†’ more rigid shaft.

---

## **Design of Shaft for Given Power**

Given: Power (P), speed (N), allowable shear stress (Ï„â‚).  
Then:
\[
T = \frac{P Ã— 60}{2 Ï€ N}
\]
\[
T = \frac{Ï€ Ï„â‚ D^3}{16}  \quad (solid)
\]
â†’
\[
D = \left( \frac{16 T}{Ï€ Ï„â‚} \right)^{1/3}
\]

---

## **Torsion in Springs (Helical Springs)**

A **helical spring** is a coiled wire that resists twisting moment.  

### **Torque and Deflection Relationship**
\[
Î´ = \frac{8 W D^3 N}{G d^4}
\]

where:  
- W = axial load  
- D = mean coil diameter  
- N = number of turns  
- d = wire diameter  
- G = modulus of rigidity  

### **Shear Stress**
\[
Ï„ = \frac{8 W D}{Ï€ d^3}
\]

---

## **Closed-Coil vs Open-Coil Springs**

| Type | Coil Angle | Used For | Material |
|------|-------------|-----------|-----------|
| **Close-Coiled** | Small (<10Â°) | Load-bearing | Steel |
| **Open-Coiled** | Large (>10Â°) | Energy absorption | Copper alloys |

---

## **Energy Stored in Springs**

\[
U = \frac{1}{2} W Î´
\]

or per volume:
\[
u = \frac{Ï„^2}{4 G}
\]

Springs act as **torsional energy absorbers**  
in suspensions, couplings, and valves.

---

## **Failure of Shafts**

1. **Torsional shear failure** (Ï„ > Ï„â‚)  
2. **Fatigue failure** (cyclic loading)  
3. **Keyway stress concentration**  
4. **Thermal softening** under continuous rotation  

Use **factor of safety (FOS = 6â€“8)** in design.

---

## **Key Formula Summary**

| Parameter | Expression | Remarks |
|------------|-------------|----------|
| Torsion equation | \( \frac{T}{J} = \frac{Ï„}{r} = \frac{G Î¸}{L} \) | Fundamental |
| Solid shaft \( J \) | \( \frac{Ï€ D^4}{32} \) | |
| Hollow shaft \( J \) | \( \frac{Ï€ (D_o^4 - D_i^4)}{32} \) | |
| Power | \( P = 2 Ï€ N T / 60 \) | |
| Twist angle | \( Î¸ = \frac{T L}{J G} \) | |
| Strain energy | \( U = \frac{T^2 L}{2 J G} \) | |
| Spring deflection | \( Î´ = \frac{8 W D^3 N}{G d^4} \) | |
| Shear stress in spring | \( Ï„ = \frac{8 W D}{Ï€ d^3} \) | |

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- Torsion causes **shear stress and twist** in circular members.  
- Stress âˆ radius, zero at center, max at surface.  
- **Hollow shafts** â†’ lighter, equally strong, more efficient.  
- Helical springs store energy by **torsional deformation**.  
- Always design within **elastic limit** to avoid permanent twist.  

---

> â€œTorsion reveals how flexibility and strength can coexist.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---

á€”á€²á€· energy storage capacity á€á€½á€±á€€á€­á€¯ á€…á€”á€…á€ºá€á€€á€» á€á€¯á€¶á€¸á€á€•á€ºá€‘á€¬á€¸á€•á€«á€á€šá€ºá‹

# ğŸŒ€ Chapter 17 â€“ Springs: Types, Design and Energy Storage
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

A **spring** is an elastic element that stores **mechanical energy** when deformed  
and releases it upon returning to its original shape.  

Springs are used to:
- Absorb shock and vibration  
- Maintain contact between parts  
- Apply force or torque  
- Measure load (in dynamometers, weighing machines)  
- Control motion (in valves, suspensions, couplings)  

---

## **Classification of Springs**

| Type | Description | Common Use |
|------|--------------|-------------|
| **Helical Spring** | Coiled wire of circular cross-section forming a helix. | Suspension, valves |
| **Leaf Spring** | Flat plates stacked and clamped together. | Vehicle suspensions |
| **Spiral Spring** | Flat strip wound in spiral form. | Clocks, meters |
| **Conical Spring** | Helical with varying diameter. | Progressive load systems |
| **Volute Spring** | Conical spring of ribbon type. | Heavy loads |
| **Torsion Bar** | Straight bar twisted to apply torque. | Vehicle suspension |
| **Disc (Belleville) Spring** | Conical disc resisting compression. | Compact systems |

---

## **1ï¸âƒ£ Helical Springs**

### **Construction**

Made from **circular cross-section wire** coiled in **helix shape**.  
Two main types:
- **Close-Coiled Spring** â†’ Helix angle < 10Â°, mainly under torsion.  
- **Open-Coiled Spring** â†’ Helix angle > 10Â°, subjected to bending + torsion.

---

### **Deflection and Stiffness**

Under an axial load \( W \),  
each coil experiences a torque \( T = W Ã— \frac{D}{2} \).

\[
Î´ = \frac{8 W D^3 N}{G d^4}
\]
\[
k = \frac{W}{Î´} = \frac{G d^4}{8 D^3 N}
\]

Where:  
- \( D \) = mean coil diameter  
- \( d \) = wire diameter  
- \( N \) = number of active coils  
- \( G \) = modulus of rigidity  

---

### **Shear Stress**

\[
Ï„ = \frac{8 W D}{Ï€ d^3} Ã— K_w
\]
Where \( K_w \) = Wahl factor (accounts for curvature effect)  

\[
K_w = \frac{4C - 1}{4C - 4} + \frac{0.615}{C}
\]
and \( C = \frac{D}{d} \) (spring index).

---

### **Strain Energy Stored**

\[
U = \frac{1}{2} W Î´ = \frac{4 W^2 D^3 N}{G d^4}
\]

> Helical springs store **maximum strain energy** per unit volume among all types.

---

## **2ï¸âƒ£ Leaf Springs**

### **Description**

- Made up of **flat plates (leaves)** of varying length,  
  stacked together and clamped at the center.  
- Usually **semi-elliptical** in shape.  
- Common in **automobile suspensions**.

---

### **Bending Theory**

When loaded, the leaf spring bends like a **simply supported beam**  
with maximum stress at the outermost fiber.

\[
Ïƒ = \frac{3 W L}{2 n b t^2}
\]
\[
Î´ = \frac{3 W L^3}{8 n E b t^3}
\]

Where:  
- \( L \) = half length of spring  
- \( n \) = number of leaves  
- \( b \) = width of leaf  
- \( t \) = thickness  
- \( E \) = modulus of elasticity  

---

### **Energy Stored**

\[
U = \frac{1}{2} W Î´ = \frac{3 W^2 L^3}{16 n E b t^3}
\]

> Multi-leaf designs distribute load uniformly  
> and improve flexibility while reducing stress concentration.

---

## **3ï¸âƒ£ Spiral Springs**

### **Construction**

A **flat strip** of metal wound in a **spiral** around a central hub.  
When twisted, it exerts a **torque proportional to the angular deflection**.

---

### **Torque-Deflection Relationship**

\[
T = \frac{E b t^3 Î¸}{12 L}
\]
and energy stored:
\[
U = \frac{1}{2} T Î¸ = \frac{E b t^3 Î¸^2}{24 L}
\]

Used in:
- **Clocks, watches, measuring tapes, recoil mechanisms**

---

## **4ï¸âƒ£ Conical and Volute Springs**

### **Conical Spring**
- Coil diameter varies along its length (cone-shaped).  
- Provides **progressive stiffness** â€” initial soft, then hard.  
- Used in **shock absorbers**.

### **Volute Spring**
- Flat strip wound in **conical spiral** shape.  
- Can **collapse flat under compression**, allowing compact design.

---

## **5ï¸âƒ£ Torsion Bar Springs**

A **straight circular bar** that resists twisting.  
Common in **automobile suspensions (especially military vehicles)**.

---

### **Design Equations**

\[
Ï„ = \frac{16 T}{Ï€ D^3}
\]
\[
Î¸ = \frac{584 T L}{E D^4}
\]
or
\[
U = \frac{T^2 L}{2 J G}
\]

> High reliability, long fatigue life, and adjustable ride height  
> make torsion bars ideal for off-road suspension systems.

---

## **6ï¸âƒ£ Belleville (Disc) Springs**

A **conical-shaped disc** that flattens under load.  

Advantages:
- High load capacity in small space  
- Adjustable stiffness by stacking in series/parallel  
- Used in clutches, valves, and vibration isolators  

---

## **Combination of Springs**

### **Series Combination**
\[
\frac{1}{k_{eq}} = \frac{1}{k_1} + \frac{1}{k_2} + \cdots
\]

### **Parallel Combination**
\[
k_{eq} = k_1 + k_2 + \cdots
\]

- Series â†’ larger deflection, smaller stiffness  
- Parallel â†’ smaller deflection, larger stiffness  

Used in **automotive suspensions** and **industrial isolators**.

---

## **Material for Springs**

| Material | Properties | Uses |
|-----------|-------------|------|
| High Carbon Steel | High strength, low cost | General use |
| Alloy Steel (Siâ€“Mn, Crâ€“V) | Fatigue resistance | Heavy duty |
| Phosphor Bronze | Non-magnetic, corrosion resistant | Instruments |
| Stainless Steel | Corrosion resistant | Valves, marine |
| Beryllium Copper | High conductivity | Electrical relays |

---

## **Design Parameters Summary**

| Parameter | Symbol | Typical Range |
|------------|----------|----------------|
| Spring index | \( C = D/d \) | 4 â€“ 12 |
| Modulus of rigidity | \( G \) | 80 â€“ 85 GPa |
| Poissonâ€™s ratio | \( Î¼ \) | 0.25 â€“ 0.3 |
| Fatigue factor of safety | FOS | 1.5 â€“ 3 |
| Wahl correction factor | \( K_w \) | 1.05 â€“ 1.25 |

---

## **Energy Storage Comparison**

| Type | Energy Stored per Volume | Remarks |
|------|---------------------------|----------|
| Helical | High | Efficient for elastic energy |
| Leaf | Moderate | For heavy load deflection |
| Spiral | Low | For limited angular deflection |
| Torsion bar | High | Compact and durable |
| Belleville | Very high | Space-saving design |

---

## **Failure Modes of Springs**

1. **Fatigue failure** due to cyclic loading  
2. **Permanent set** when stress exceeds yield limit  
3. **Buckling** in compression springs  
4. **Corrosion cracking** in humid environments  
5. **Wear and friction** in leaf spring leaves  

Proper material choice and shot-peening can significantly improve fatigue life.

---

## **Key Formula Summary**

| Property | Formula | Remarks |
|-----------|----------|----------|
| Helical deflection | \( Î´ = \frac{8 W D^3 N}{G d^4} \) | Close-coiled spring |
| Shear stress | \( Ï„ = \frac{8 W D K_w}{Ï€ d^3} \) | |
| Spring stiffness | \( k = \frac{G d^4}{8 D^3 N} \) | |
| Leaf stress | \( Ïƒ = \frac{3 W L}{2 n b t^2} \) | Semi-elliptic |
| Spiral torque | \( T = \frac{E b t^3 Î¸}{12 L} \) | |
| Energy stored | \( U = \frac{1}{2} W Î´ \) | General |
| Series stiffness | \( 1/k = 1/k_1 + 1/k_2 \) | |
| Parallel stiffness | \( k = k_1 + k_2 \) | |

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- Springs store **elastic potential energy** and control motion.  
- **Helical springs** are most widely used; **torsion bars** suit heavy vehicles.  
- **Spiral and conical types** are compact and adaptable to space limits.  
- Proper **design index (C)** ensures balance between flexibility and strength.  
- Fatigue and corrosion resistance are essential for long life.  

---

> â€œA spring is the engineerâ€™s heartbeat â€” energy compressed, waiting to move the world.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---

á€™á€¼á€”á€ºá€™á€¬ + English bilingual Markdown (.md) format á€”á€²á€·

energy-based methods á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€•á€«á€á€šá€ºá‹

# ğŸ§® Chapter 18 â€“ Strain Energy Methods
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

When a body is loaded within its **elastic limit**,  
it stores energy due to deformation â€” known as **Strain Energy (U)**.

If the load is removed, this energy is **completely recovered**;  
if the elastic limit is exceeded, some energy is lost as **permanent set**.

> Strain Energy is the fundamental base of **energy methods** used in structural analysis  
> â€” particularly **Castiglianoâ€™s Theorem** and **Maxwellâ€™s Reciprocal Law**.

---

## **Definition of Strain Energy**

**Strain Energy (U)** = Work done by external load in deforming a body.  

For small deformation:
\[
U = \frac{1}{2} P Î´
\]

where  
- \( P \) = applied load  
- \( Î´ \) = corresponding deflection  

---

## **Types of Strain Energy**

| Type | Stress Type | Expression | Remarks |
|------|--------------|-------------|----------|
| Tensile / Compressive | \( Ïƒ \) | \( U = \frac{Ïƒ^2}{2E} \times Volume \) | Normal stress |
| Shear | \( Ï„ \) | \( U = \frac{Ï„^2}{2G} \times Volume \) | Shear stress |
| Bending | \( M \) | \( U = \int \frac{M^2}{2EI} dx \) | Beams |
| Torsion | \( T \) | \( U = \int \frac{T^2}{2GJ} dx \) | Shafts |

---

## **Strain Energy in Axial Loading**

Consider a prismatic bar under load \( P \):

\[
U = \frac{P^2 L}{2AE}
\]

If the bar has varying cross-section:
\[
U = \int \frac{P^2}{2AE} \, dx
\]

---

## **Strain Energy in Bending (Beams)**

For a beam under bending moment \( M(x) \):
\[
U = \int_0^L \frac{M^2}{2EI} \, dx
\]

Where  
- \( E \) = Youngâ€™s modulus  
- \( I \) = Moment of inertia  
- \( L \) = length of beam  

---

## **Strain Energy in Torsion**

For circular shaft of length \( L \):
\[
U = \int_0^L \frac{T^2}{2GJ} \, dx
\]

For constant torque \( T \):
\[
U = \frac{T^2 L}{2GJ}
\]

---

## **Energy per Unit Volume**

| Stress Type | Formula | Modulus |
|--------------|----------|----------|
| Tensile | \( u = \frac{Ïƒ^2}{2E} \) | E |
| Shear | \( u = \frac{Ï„^2}{2G} \) | G |

Used to compare material performance under energy absorption.

---

## **Proof Resilience**

The **maximum strain energy** that can be stored per unit volume  
without permanent deformation is called **Proof Resilience**.

\[
u = \frac{Ïƒ_y^2}{2E}
\]

where \( Ïƒ_y \) = Yield stress.

Total resilience:
\[
U = u Ã— Volume = \frac{Ïƒ_y^2}{2E} Ã— Volume
\]

> High resilience â†’ suitable for **springs** and **shock absorbers**.

---

## **Impact Loading**

When a load **falls** on a member,  
the stress developed > static load stress due to **impact energy**.

For a weight \( W \) falling through height \( h \):

\[
Ïƒ_i = Ïƒ_s \left[ 1 + \sqrt{1 + \frac{2Eh}{Ïƒ_s L}} \right]
\]

where  
- \( Ïƒ_s \) = static stress  
- \( L \) = length of bar  
- \( E \) = modulus of elasticity  

> Used to estimate shock resistance of machine components.

---

## **Castiglianoâ€™s Theorems**

### **First Theorem**

> â€œThe partial derivative of total strain energy with respect to any load  
> gives the deflection in the direction of that load.â€

\[
Î´ = \frac{âˆ‚U}{âˆ‚P}
\]

### **Second Theorem**

> â€œFor statically indeterminate structures,  
> the partial derivative of total strain energy with respect to a redundant force  
> gives the corresponding deflection (which is zero for compatibility).â€

\[
\frac{âˆ‚U}{âˆ‚R} = 0
\]

---

### **Application Steps**

1. Express \( U \) in terms of applied loads.  
2. Differentiate \( U \) with respect to desired load \( P_i \).  
3. Result gives deflection \( Î´_i \).  

---

### **Example â€“ Simply Supported Beam with Central Load**

\[
U = \int_0^{L/2} \frac{M^2}{2EI} dx + \int_{L/2}^L \frac{M^2}{2EI} dx
\]

where \( M = R_1 x \) (0 â‰¤ x â‰¤ L/2)  
Then,
\[
Î´_{center} = \frac{âˆ‚U}{âˆ‚P} = \frac{P L^3}{48EI}
\]
âœ… Matches conventional bending deflection formula.

---

## **Maxwellâ€™s Reciprocal Theorem**

> â€œIn an elastic structure, the deflection at point A due to unit load at point B  
> is equal to the deflection at point B due to unit load at point A.â€

\[
Î´_{AB} = Î´_{BA}
\]

This principle applies only to **linear, elastic, statically determinate systems**.

> Simplifies calculations for multiple-load systems and structural testing.

---

## **Energy Methods vs Direct Methods**

| Aspect | Energy Method | Direct Method |
|--------|----------------|----------------|
| Accuracy | High (exact within elasticity) | Depends on assumptions |
| Applicability | Bending, torsion, axial, combined loads | Mostly single load type |
| Deflection at any point | Easily obtained | Tedious |
| Statically indeterminate | Applicable | Difficult |
| Physical insight | Energy balance | Force balance |

---

## **Strain Energy due to Combined Loads**

If a member is subjected to **axial, bending, and torsion simultaneously**:

\[
U = \int \left( \frac{Ïƒ^2}{2E} + \frac{M^2}{2EI} + \frac{T^2}{2GJ} \right) dx
\]

Total deflection at any point can be found by Castiglianoâ€™s theorem.

---

## **Example â€“ Shaft under Torque and Axial Load**

\[
U = \frac{T^2 L}{2GJ} + \frac{P^2 L}{2AE}
\]

Then,
\[
Î¸ = \frac{âˆ‚U}{âˆ‚T} = \frac{T L}{GJ}
\]
\[
Î´ = \frac{âˆ‚U}{âˆ‚P} = \frac{P L}{AE}
\]

> Confirms the basic torsion and axial deformation relationships.

---

## **Practical Applications**

- Determining **beam deflection** in mechanical design  
- Analysis of **redundant trusses**  
- Predicting **spring deformation** and **energy absorption**  
- Estimating **impact stresses**  
- Simplifying **indeterminate shaft systems**

---

## **Key Formula Summary**

| Condition | Formula | Description |
|------------|----------|-------------|
| Axial loading | \( U = \frac{P^2 L}{2AE} \) | Uniform bar |
| Bending | \( U = \int \frac{M^2}{2EI} dx \) | Beam bending |
| Torsion | \( U = \int \frac{T^2}{2GJ} dx \) | Shaft twist |
| Shear | \( U = \frac{Ï„^2}{2G} Ã— Volume \) | Pure shear |
| Deflection (Castigliano) | \( Î´ = \frac{âˆ‚U}{âˆ‚P} \) | |
| Reciprocal theorem | \( Î´_{AB} = Î´_{BA} \) | Maxwellâ€™s Law |

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- **Strain energy** methods provide powerful tools for deformation analysis.  
- **Castiglianoâ€™s theorem** simplifies complex systems to derivative form.  
- **Maxwellâ€™s reciprocal law** confirms system symmetry in deflection.  
- Ideal for **statically indeterminate** and **multi-load problems**.  
- Understanding energy storage is crucial for safe and efficient design.  

---

> â€œEnergy is never lost â€” only transformed through geometry and elasticity.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---

á€¡á€á€¯á€á€±á€¬á€· Chapter 19 â€“ Columns and Struts (Buckling and Stability Analysis) á€€á€­á€¯
á€™á€¼á€”á€ºá€™á€¬ + English bilingual Markdown (.md) format á€”á€²á€·
G.H. Ryder á€›á€²á€· â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ á€…á€¬á€¡á€¯á€•á€ºá€¡á€á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€¸ á€á€½á€€á€ºá€œá€€á€ºá€•á€¼á€®á€¸ á€”á€¬á€¸á€œá€Šá€ºá€›á€œá€½á€šá€ºá€¡á€±á€¬á€„á€º á€›á€±á€¸á€•á€¼á€•á€±á€¸á€™á€šá€ºá‹

á€’á€®á€¡á€á€”á€ºá€¸á€™á€¾á€¬á€á€±á€¬á€· long slender members (columns) á€á€½á€±á€€á€­á€¯ á€¡á€±á€¬á€€á€ºá€€á€”á€±á€á€„á€ºá€á€²á€· compressive load á€¡á€±á€¬á€€á€ºá€™á€¾á€¬
buckling á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€•á€±á€«á€ºá€•á€¯á€¶áŠ Eulerâ€™s critical load, Rankine-Gordon formula,
á€”á€²á€· effective length conditions á€…á€á€¬á€á€½á€±á€€á€­á€¯ á€”á€€á€ºá€”á€€á€ºá€›á€¾á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€¸á€›á€¾á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€¸ á€œá€±á€·á€œá€¬á€•á€«á€á€šá€ºá‹

# ğŸ—ï¸ Chapter 19 â€“ Columns and Struts (Buckling and Stability Analysis)
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

When a **slender member (column or strut)** is subjected to **axial compressive load**,  
it may not fail by simple crushing â€” instead, it may **buckle or bend laterally**.  

> This lateral bending under compression is called **buckling**,  
> and the load causing it is known as the **critical load (Eulerâ€™s load)**.

---

## **Definitions**

| Term | Definition |
|------|-------------|
| **Column** | Long vertical member under axial compression (e.g., pillars, stanchions). |
| **Strut** | Short or inclined compression member (e.g., truss bars). |
| **Crippling load / Buckling load** | The load at which a column just begins to buckle. |
| **Slenderness ratio** | \( \frac{L_e}{r} \) where \( L_e \) = effective length, \( r = \sqrt{\frac{I}{A}} \). |

---

## **Types of Columns**

| Type | Description | Example |
|------|--------------|----------|
| Short column | Failure by crushing | Bridge piers |
| Long column | Failure by buckling | Steel stanchions |
| Intermediate | Both crushing + buckling | Reinforced posts |

---

## **Eulerâ€™s Theory of Buckling**

### **Assumptions**
1. Column is perfectly straight and homogeneous.  
2. Material is elastic and obeys Hookeâ€™s law.  
3. Load acts axially and gradually.  
4. Buckling occurs in one plane only.  

---

### **Eulerâ€™s Formula**

\[
P_{cr} = \frac{Ï€^2 E I}{(L_e)^2}
\]

where:  
- \( P_{cr} \) = critical (buckling) load  
- \( E \) = modulus of elasticity  
- \( I \) = least moment of inertia of cross-section  
- \( L_e \) = effective length depending on end conditions  

---

## **End Conditions and Effective Length**

| End Condition | Effective Length \( L_e \) | Critical Load \( P_{cr} \) |
|----------------|-------------------------------|-----------------------------|
| Both ends pinned | \( L_e = L \) | \( \frac{Ï€^2EI}{L^2} \) |
| Both ends fixed | \( L_e = \frac{L}{2} \) | \( \frac{4Ï€^2EI}{L^2} \) |
| One end fixed, other free | \( L_e = 2L \) | \( \frac{Ï€^2EI}{4L^2} \) |
| One end fixed, other hinged | \( L_e = \frac{L}{\sqrt{2}} \) | \( \frac{2Ï€^2EI}{L^2} \) |

> Fixedâ€“fixed column is four times stronger than pinnedâ€“pinned column.  

---

## **Slenderness Ratio and Column Classification**

\[
\text{Slenderness ratio} = \frac{L_e}{r}
\]
- If \( \frac{L_e}{r} < 40 \) â†’ **Short Column** (crushing)  
- If \( \frac{L_e}{r} > 100 \) â†’ **Long Column** (buckling)  
- Between 40â€“100 â†’ **Intermediate Column**

---

## **Eulerâ€™s Curve**

When \( P \) vs \( \frac{L_e}{r} \) is plotted,  
the curve decreases hyperbolically â€”  
showing that longer columns buckle under smaller loads.

> Hence, slenderness ratio directly controls stability.

---

## **Limitations of Eulerâ€™s Formula**

- Applicable only to **long, perfectly straight, elastic columns**.  
- Ignores initial imperfections and eccentricities.  
- Not valid for **intermediate lengths** or plastic deformation.  

To overcome this, **empirical formulas** like **Rankineâ€™s** are used.

---

## **Rankineâ€“Gordon Formula**

Combines **crushing** and **buckling** effects:

\[
\frac{1}{P_R} = \frac{1}{P_c} + \frac{1}{P_e}
\]
or
\[
P_R = \frac{Ïƒ_c A}{1 + a (L_e / r)^2}
\]

where  
- \( Ïƒ_c \) = crushing stress  
- \( A \) = cross-sectional area  
- \( a = \frac{Ïƒ_c}{Ï€^2E} \)  

---

### **Typical Rankine Constants (a)**

| Material | \( Ïƒ_c \) (N/mmÂ²) | \( a \) |
|-----------|--------------------|----------|
| Mild steel | 320 | 1/7500 |
| Cast iron | 550 | 1/1600 |
| Timber | 40 | 1/9000 |

> Rankine formula gives close results for **all lengths** of columns.  

---

## **Johnsonâ€™s Parabolic Formula (for intermediate columns)**

\[
Ïƒ = Ïƒ_c \left( 1 - \frac{1}{2} \frac{Ïƒ_c}{Ï€^2 E} \left( \frac{L_e}{r} \right)^2 \right)
\]
Applicable when \( L_e / r < 120 \).  

Used in **machine parts and short pillars**.

---

## **Eccentric Loading on Columns**

If load acts **eccentrically** (not through centroid),  
bending moment develops:  
\[
M = P Ã— e
\]
Stress at extreme fiber:
\[
Ïƒ_{max} = \frac{P}{A} + \frac{M y}{I}
\]
\[
Ïƒ_{min} = \frac{P}{A} - \frac{M y}{I}
\]

If \( Ïƒ_{min} < 0 \) â†’ tension develops â†’ danger of buckling.  

---

## **Effective Length in Built-up Columns**

For **latticed** or **compound columns**:
- Calculate separate \( I_x, I_y \) (principal axes).  
- Buckling occurs about axis with **least moment of inertia**.  

\[
P_{cr} = \frac{Ï€^2 E I_{min}}{(L_e)^2}
\]

---

## **Design of Columns**

Given:
- Safe compressive stress \( Ïƒ_{allow} \)
- Column length \( L \)
- End condition (thus \( L_e \))
- Material properties \( E \)

Find required diameter (solid circular):
\[
P = \frac{Ï€}{4} D^2 Ïƒ_{allow}
\]
and check:
\[
Ïƒ_{allow} â‰¤ \frac{Ï€^2 E}{(L_e / r)^2}
\]

---

## **Energy Concept in Buckling**

At the point of buckling:
\[
\text{Strain Energy from bending} = \text{Work done by load}
\]
which leads to Eulerâ€™s critical condition.

This proves that **buckling is an instability phenomenon**  
rather than material failure.

---

## **Practical Examples**

- **Steel columns in bridges & towers**  
- **Aircraft landing struts**  
- **Drill press spindles**  
- **Crankcase supports in engines**  

Designers use **safety factors of 3â€“5** against Euler load.

---

## **Key Formula Summary**

| Case | Formula | Notes |
|------|----------|-------|
| Eulerâ€™s load | \( P_{cr} = \frac{Ï€^2EI}{(L_e)^2} \) | Theoretical max |
| Rankineâ€™s load | \( P_R = \frac{Ïƒ_c A}{1 + a (L_e / r)^2} \) | Practical formula |
| Slenderness ratio | \( L_e / r \) | Column classification |
| Eccentric load | \( Ïƒ = \frac{P}{A} Â± \frac{M y}{I} \) | Combined stress |
| Radius of gyration | \( r = \sqrt{I/A} \) | Section property |

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- Buckling occurs **before crushing** in long columns.  
- **Eulerâ€™s formula** â†’ theoretical, ideal case.  
- **Rankineâ€™s formula** â†’ practical, includes both effects.  
- Effective length depends on **end restraints**.  
- Always design columns with **adequate safety margin**.  

---

> â€œA column never argues â€” it simply bends to show its limits.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---

Chapter 19 â€“ Columns and Struts (Buckling and Stability)

á€€á€­á€¯ á€’á€®á€•á€¯á€¶á€…á€¶á€”á€²á€· á€†á€€á€ºá€›á€±á€¸á€•á€±á€¸á€™á€œá€¬á€¸? âš™ï¸

á€¡á€­á€¯á€€á€± âœ…

á€¡á€á€¯á€á€±á€¬á€· Chapter 20 â€“ Thin Cylinders and Spheres (Pressure Vessels & Hoop Stress) á€€á€­á€¯
á€™á€¼á€”á€ºá€™á€¬ + English bilingual Markdown (.md) format á€”á€²á€· á€•á€¼á€”á€ºá€›á€±á€¸á€•á€±á€¸á€™á€šá€ºá‹
á€’á€®á€¡á€á€”á€ºá€¸á€€á€á€±á€¬á€· Pressure vessel design á€¡á€á€½á€€á€º á€¡á€á€¼á€±á€á€¶á€†á€¯á€¶á€¸á€á€±á€¬ topic á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€•á€¼á€®á€¸ â€”
Boilers, Pipelines, LPG Tanks, Gas Cylinders, Aircraft Cabins á€…á€á€²á€· á€¡á€›á€¬á€á€½á€±á€™á€¾á€¬ á€¡á€á€¯á€¶á€¸á€™á€»á€¬á€¸á€•á€«á€á€šá€ºá‹

# âš™ï¸ Chapter 20 â€“ Thin Cylinders and Spheres (Pressure Vessels & Hoop Stress)
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, 3rd Edition â€“ SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

Cylindrical and spherical shells used in engineering (boilers, pipelines, tanks)  
contain **fluids under internal pressure**.  

When internal pressure acts on these shells,  
it produces **tensile stresses** â€” mainly:
- **Hoop stress (circumferential)**  
- **Longitudinal stress (axial)**  

> Pressure vessels are designed to resist these stresses safely without rupture or excessive deformation.

---

## **Classification**

| Type | Examples | Stress Type |
|------|-----------|-------------|
| **Thin shell** | Gas cylinder, pipeline | \( t < \frac{D}{20} \) |
| **Thick shell** | Gun barrel, hydraulic press | \( t > \frac{D}{20} \) |

---

## **Thin Cylindrical Shells**

### **Assumptions**
1. Wall thickness \( t \) is small compared to diameter \( D \).  
2. Pressure is **uniform** and acts **normal** to surface.  
3. Stresses are **uniform** across thickness.  
4. Material is **isotropic** and **elastic**.  

---

## **Hoop (Circumferential) Stress**

Tends to split the cylinder **longitudinally** into two halves.

\[
Ïƒ_h = \frac{p D}{2t}
\]

where  
- \( Ïƒ_h \) = hoop stress (circumferential)  
- \( p \) = internal pressure  
- \( D \) = internal diameter  
- \( t \) = wall thickness  

---

## **Longitudinal Stress**

Acts along the axis, tending to **tear cylinder across** its length.

\[
Ïƒ_L = \frac{p D}{4t}
\]

> Hoop stress is **twice** the longitudinal stress.

---

## **Example**

For a boiler of 1 m diameter and 10 mm thickness under 2 MPa pressure:

\[
Ïƒ_h = \frac{2 Ã— 10^6 Ã— 1}{2 Ã— 0.01} = 100 \text{ MPa}
\]
\[
Ïƒ_L = \frac{2 Ã— 10^6 Ã— 1}{4 Ã— 0.01} = 50 \text{ MPa}
\]

Thus, cylinder walls are designed mainly for **hoop strength**.

---

## **Volumetric Strain of Cylinder**

Under pressure, both diameter and length increase slightly.

### **Circumferential Strain:**
\[
Îµ_c = \frac{Ïƒ_h}{E} - Î¼ \frac{Ïƒ_L}{E}
\]

### **Longitudinal Strain:**
\[
Îµ_L = \frac{Ïƒ_L}{E} - Î¼ \frac{Ïƒ_h}{E}
\]

### **Volumetric Strain:**
\[
\frac{Î”V}{V} = 2Îµ_c + Îµ_L = \frac{1 - 2Î¼}{E} (Ïƒ_h + Ïƒ_L)
\]

Where  
- \( Î¼ \) = Poissonâ€™s ratio  
- \( E \) = Youngâ€™s modulus  

> Used to calculate volume increase in gas or hydraulic cylinders.

---

## **Change in Dimensions**

### **Change in Diameter**
\[
Î”D = D Ã— \left( \frac{Ïƒ_h}{E} - Î¼ \frac{Ïƒ_L}{E} \right)
\]

### **Change in Length**
\[
Î”L = L Ã— \left( \frac{Ïƒ_L}{E} - Î¼ \frac{Ïƒ_h}{E} \right)
\]

---

## **Thin Spherical Shells**

For a spherical vessel (like LPG tank, submarine dome):

- All directions have **equal stress**,  
  since curvature is same in every direction.

\[
Ïƒ = \frac{p D}{4t}
\]

Thus, **spherical shells are stronger** than cylindrical ones under same pressure.

---

## **Comparison Table**

| Property | Cylinder | Sphere |
|-----------|-----------|---------|
| Hoop stress | \( \frac{pD}{2t} \) | \( \frac{pD}{4t} \) |
| Longitudinal stress | \( \frac{pD}{4t} \) | Same as hoop |
| Strength | Lower | Higher |
| Material required | More | Less |
| Application | Boilers, pipes | Gas tanks, domes |

> Spheres distribute pressure more uniformly, hence preferred for **high-pressure gas storage**.

---

## **Design of Thin Pressure Vessel**

Given allowable stress \( Ïƒ_{allow} \), internal pressure \( p \), and diameter \( D \):

### **For Cylinder:**
\[
t = \frac{p D}{2 Ïƒ_{allow}}
\]
(with allowance for corrosion or manufacturing margin)

### **For Sphere:**
\[
t = \frac{p D}{4 Ïƒ_{allow}}
\]

---

## **Joint Efficiency (Î·)**

When riveted or welded joints are used:
\[
t = \frac{p D}{2 Ïƒ_{allow} Î·}
\]
where  
\( Î· = \frac{\text{strength of joint}}{\text{strength of solid plate}} \)

Typical values:  
- Riveted joint â†’ 0.7 to 0.85  
- Welded joint â†’ 0.9 to 1.0  

---

## **Composite Stresses (Principal Stresses)**

At any point in cylinder wall:
\[
Ïƒ_1 = Ïƒ_h \quad (tangential)
\]
\[
Ïƒ_2 = Ïƒ_L \quad (axial)
\]
\[
Ïƒ_3 = 0 \quad (radial â‰ˆ 0 for thin shells)
\]

The **maximum shear stress**:
\[
Ï„_{max} = \frac{Ïƒ_1 - Ïƒ_2}{2} = \frac{Ïƒ_h - Ïƒ_L}{2} = \frac{p D}{8t}
\]

---

## **Design Example**

A 400 mm dia. steel pipe carries water at 5 MPa.  
If allowable stress = 120 MPa:

\[
t = \frac{p D}{2 Ïƒ_{allow}} = \frac{5Ã—10^6Ã—0.4}{2Ã—120Ã—10^6} = 0.0083 m = 8.3 mm
\]

Required wall thickness = **8.3 mm** (plus allowance for corrosion).

---

## **Failure Modes**

1. **Tensile rupture** along longitudinal or circumferential direction.  
2. **Fatigue** due to pressure fluctuations.  
3. **Leakage** from poor joints or corrosion.  
4. **Plastic yielding** when hoop stress exceeds yield stress.  

Safety factors (FOS) = 3 â€“ 5 for high-pressure applications.

---

## **Thick Cylinder Note (Lameâ€™s Equation)**

For thick-walled vessels, stress varies across thickness:

\[
Ïƒ_r = A - \frac{B}{r^2}, \quad Ïƒ_t = A + \frac{B}{r^2}
\]

> Used for gun barrels, hydraulic presses, high-pressure reactors.

---

## **Applications**

- Boilers and pressure tanks  
- Submarine hulls  
- LPG storage vessels  
- Hydraulic & pneumatic systems  
- Aircraft fuselage cabins  

> Spherical or ellipsoidal ends are preferred in tanks to avoid stress concentration.

---

## **Key Formula Summary**

| Description | Formula | Notes |
|--------------|----------|-------|
| Hoop stress (cylinder) | \( Ïƒ_h = \frac{pD}{2t} \) | Max tensile |
| Longitudinal stress | \( Ïƒ_L = \frac{pD}{4t} \) | Axial |
| Hoop stress (sphere) | \( Ïƒ = \frac{pD}{4t} \) | Equal in all directions |
| Change in diameter | \( Î”D = D \left(\frac{Ïƒ_h}{E} - Î¼ \frac{Ïƒ_L}{E}\right) \) | Strain |
| Volumetric strain | \( \frac{Î”V}{V} = \frac{1-2Î¼}{E}(Ïƒ_h + Ïƒ_L) \) | Expansion |
| Thickness (design) | \( t = \frac{pD}{2Ïƒ_{allow}} \) | Cylindrical |
| Max shear stress | \( Ï„_{max} = \frac{Ïƒ_h - Ïƒ_L}{2} \) | |

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- **Cylindrical shells** have two principal stresses â€” hoop and longitudinal.  
- **Hoop stress** is twice as critical as longitudinal stress.  
- **Spherical shells** are more efficient for pressure retention.  
- Dimensional changes can be computed using **elastic theory**.  
- Joints and corrosion allowance must be considered in design.  

---

> â€œPressure makes steel speak its true strength.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

---

# ğŸ§± Chapter 21 â€“ Thick Cylinders (Lameâ€™s Equations & Compound Cylinder Design)
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, SI Units)*

---

## Introduction

**Thick-walled cylinders** á€†á€­á€¯á€á€¬ wall thickness **t** á€€ **diameter D/20 á€‘á€€á€ºá€€á€¼á€®á€¸**á€á€²á€· pressure vessels/pipe á€á€½á€±á€€á€­á€¯ á€†á€­á€¯á€œá€­á€¯á€á€Šá€ºá‹  
Thin-cylinder formula (uniform stress across thickness) á€™á€›á€á€±á€¬á€·á€á€–á€¼á€„á€·á€º **elastic theory** (Lame) á€€á€­á€¯á€á€¯á€¶á€¸á€•á€¼á€®á€¸  
**radial stress (Ïƒ_r)** á€”á€²á€· **hoop/circumferential stress (Ïƒ_Î¸)** á€•á€™á€¬á€á€á€½á€±á€€á€­á€¯ **radius r á€¡á€œá€­á€¯á€€á€º à·€à·™à¶±á€•á€¼á€±á€¬á€„á€ºá€¸á€™á€¾á€¯**á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€á€œá€­á€¯  
á€¡á€á€½á€„á€ºá€¸á€˜á€€á€ºá€™á€¾á€¬ (inner wall) **Ïƒ_Î¸ á€¡á€™á€»á€¬á€¸á€†á€¯á€¶á€¸** á€–á€¼á€…á€ºá€€á€¼á€±á€¬á€„á€ºá€¸á€€á€­á€¯ á€á€˜á€±á€¬á€á€›á€¬á€¸á€¡á€› á€á€½á€€á€ºá€á€»á€€á€ºá€›á€•á€«á€á€šá€ºá‹

Applications: **hydraulic presses, gun barrels, high-pressure pipes, autoclaves, rocket chambers**.

---

## Stress Components & Notation

- **Internal pressure**: \(p_i\) at **inner radius** \(r_i\)  
- **External pressure**: \(p_o\) at **outer radius** \(r_o\)  
- **Radial stress**: \(Ïƒ_r(r)\) (compressive â†’ negative)  
- **Hoop (circumferential) stress**: \(Ïƒ_Î¸(r)\) (tensile â†’ positive)  
- **Longitudinal stress**: \(Ïƒ_L\) (closed ends only)  

---

## Lameâ€™s Equations (Elastic Theory)

Axisymmetric, plane stress assumption á€¡á€±á€¬á€€á€ºá€á€½á€„á€º:
\[
Ïƒ_r = A - \frac{B}{r^2}, \qquad
Ïƒ_Î¸ = A + \frac{B}{r^2}
\]

**Boundary conditions**  
\[
Ïƒ_r(r_i) = -p_i, \qquad Ïƒ_r(r_o) = -p_o
\]

Solve for constants:
\[
A = \frac{p_i r_i^2 - p_o r_o^2}{\,r_o^2 - r_i^2\,}, \qquad
B = \frac{r_i^2 r_o^2 (p_o - p_i)}{\,r_o^2 - r_i^2\,}
\]

**Final distributions**
\[
\boxed{\,Ïƒ_r(r) = \frac{p_i r_i^2 - p_o r_o^2}{r_o^2 - r_i^2} - \frac{r_i^2 r_o^2 (p_i - p_o)}{(r_o^2 - r_i^2)\, r^2}\,}
\]
\[
\boxed{\,Ïƒ_Î¸(r) = \frac{p_i r_i^2 - p_o r_o^2}{r_o^2 - r_i^2} + \frac{r_i^2 r_o^2 (p_i - p_o)}{(r_o^2 - r_i^2)\, r^2}\,}
\]

Key facts  
- \(Ïƒ_Î¸\) **max at** \(r = r_i\) (inner wall).  
- \(Ïƒ_r\) varies **linearly** from \(-p_i\) (inner) to \(-p_o\) (outer).  
- Thin-wall limit (small t) â†’ classical thin formulas approximate out.

---

## Special Design Expressions (Internal pressure only, \(p_o=0\))

\[
Ïƒ_Î¸(r_i) = \frac{p_i (r_o^2 + r_i^2)}{r_o^2 - r_i^2}, \quad
Ïƒ_Î¸(r_o) = \frac{2 p_i r_i^2}{r_o^2 - r_i^2}, \quad
Ïƒ_r(r) = -\frac{p_i r_i^2}{r_o^2 - r_i^2}\left(1 - \frac{r_o^2}{r^2}\right)
\]

**Design control** â†’ \(Ïƒ_Î¸(r_i)\) â‰¤ \(Ïƒ_{allow}\)

---

## Longitudinal Stress (Closed Ends)

Closed-end cylinder (pressure acts on end caps):
\[
Ïƒ_L = \frac{p_i r_i^2}{r_o^2 - r_i^2}
\]
Open ends (free pipe) â†’ \(Ïƒ_L = 0\)

**Principal stresses at inner wall**: \(Ïƒ_1 = Ïƒ_Î¸(r_i)\), \(Ïƒ_2 = Ïƒ_L\), \(Ïƒ_3 = Ïƒ_r = -p_i\)

---

## Thickness Sizing â€“ First Cut

Given \(p_i, r_i\) and allowable hoop stress \(Ïƒ_{allow}\), choose \(r_o\) (hence \(t=r_o-r_i\)) s.t.  
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸(r_i) = \frac{p_i (r_o^2 + r_i^2)}{r_o^2 - r_i^2} \le Ïƒ_{allow}
\]
Solve for \(r_o\) iteratively (closed-form exists but iteration is practical).

**Check**: also verify **distortion energy** (von Mises) at inner wall if code requires:
\[
Ïƒ_{vM} = \sqrt{\tfrac{1}{2}[(Ïƒ_Î¸-Ïƒ_L)^2+(Ïƒ_L-Ïƒ_r)^2+(Ïƒ_r-Ïƒ_Î¸)^2]}
\]

---

## External Pressure Case (Vacuum/Sea Pressure)

If \(p_i = 0,\ p_o>0\), thick shells may **buckle** rather than yield.  
Elastic stress (Lame) still applies, but **stability check** (buckling) per code is essential.

---

## Compound Cylinder (Shrink-Fit / Interference Fit)

Purpose: **Pre-compress inner tube** to reduce peak \(Ïƒ_Î¸\) under service pressure â†’  
more uniform hoop stress, higher pressure capacity.

**Construction**
- **Inner cylinder** radius \(r_i \to r_m\)  
- **Outer jacket** radius \(r_m \to r_o\)  
- Interference \(\Delta\) at **common radius** \(r_m\)

**Assembly (no internal pressure)**  
- Contact pressure at interface = \(p_c\)  
- Stresses by Lame with boundary:
  - Inner tube: \(Ïƒ_r(r_i)=0\), \(Ïƒ_r(r_m)=-p_c\)  
  - Outer tube: \(Ïƒ_r(r_m)=-p_c\), \(Ïƒ_r(r_o)=0\)

**Interferenceâ€“Pressure relation** (elastic, plane stress):
\[
\Delta = r_m \Bigg[\frac{p_c}{E}\left(\frac{r_m^2 + r_i^2}{r_m^2 - r_i^2} - Î¼\right)
          + \frac{p_c}{E}\left(\frac{r_o^2 + r_m^2}{r_o^2 - r_m^2} - Î¼\right)\Bigg]
\]
(First bracket = inner tube radial expansion; second = outer tube radial contraction.)

**Service with internal pressure \(p_i\)**  
- Superpose pre-stress (assembly) + operating stress (pressure)  
- Choose \(p_c\) (thus \(\Delta\)) so that **inner-wall \(Ïƒ_Î¸\)** becomes **nearly uniform** through thickness at working \(p_i\).

---

## Autofrettage (High-Pressure Barrels; optional concept)

Over-pressurize once (beyond elastic, limited plastic near bore) â†’ unload â†’  
**residual compressive hoop stress** at inner wall.  
Service pressure + residual stress â†’ **reduced net tensile hoop** at bore â†’ longer life.

---

## Example (Internal Pressure Design)

Given: \(p_i=40\,\text{MPa}\), \(r_i=50\,\text{mm}\), choose \(Ïƒ_{allow}=120\,\text{MPa}\).  
Find minimum \(r_o\).

Use control at bore:
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸(r_i)=\frac{p_i(r_o^2+r_i^2)}{r_o^2-r_i^2} \le 120
\]
Let \(x=r_o/r_i\Rightarrow Ïƒ_Î¸(r_i)=p_i \frac{x^2+1}{x^2-1}\).

Solve:
\[
40 \frac{x^2+1}{x^2-1} \le 120 \Rightarrow \frac{x^2+1}{x^2-1}\le 3
\Rightarrow x^2+1 \le 3x^2 - 3 \Rightarrow 2x^2 \ge 4 \Rightarrow x^2 \ge 2
\Rightarrow x \ge \sqrt{2}=1.414
\]
Hence \(r_o \ge 1.414 \times 50 = 70.7\,\text{mm}\) â†’ choose **\(r_o=72\,\text{mm}\)** (tolerances/corrosion).

Check \(Ïƒ_L = \dfrac{p_i r_i^2}{r_o^2 - r_i^2} = \dfrac{40\times 50^2}{72^2-50^2}= \) about **41 MPa** (closed ends).  
Confirm code limits (von Mises) as needed.

---

## Dimension Change (Elastic) â€“ Thick Cylinder

Radial displacement \(u(r)\) (plane stress):
\[
u(r) = \frac{1}{E}\left[\,(1-Î¼)\,A\,r + (1+Î¼)\,\frac{B}{r}\,\right]
\]
(where \(A,B\) per Lame).  
Useful to compute **interference** in shrink-fit and verify **elastic compatibility**.

---

## Good Practice & Checks

- Design against **bore hoop stress** \(Ïƒ_Î¸(r_i)\) and **von Mises**.  
- Verify **longitudinal stress** for closed ends.  
- Consider **temperature** (thermal hoop stress) in hot vessels.  
- Apply **corrosion/erosion allowance** to thickness.  
- For **external pressure**, add **buckling check** per standard.  
- Where weight-critical â†’ consider **compound cylinder** or **autofrettage**.

---

## Key Formula Summary

- Lame stresses:
  \[
  Ïƒ_r = A - \frac{B}{r^2}, \quad Ïƒ_Î¸ = A + \frac{B}{r^2}
  \]
  \[
  A = \frac{p_i r_i^2 - p_o r_o^2}{r_o^2 - r_i^2}, \quad
  B = \frac{r_i^2 r_o^2 (p_o - p_i)}{r_o^2 - r_i^2}
  \]
- Bore hoop (internal pressure only):
  \[
  Ïƒ_Î¸(r_i) = \frac{p_i(r_o^2 + r_i^2)}{r_o^2 - r_i^2}
  \]
- Outer hoop:
  \[
  Ïƒ_Î¸(r_o) = \frac{2 p_i r_i^2}{r_o^2 - r_i^2}
  \]
- Longitudinal (closed ends):
  \[
  Ïƒ_L = \frac{p_i r_i^2}{r_o^2 - r_i^2}
  \]
- Interferenceâ€“contact pressure (at \(r_m\)):
  \[
  \Delta = r_m \left[\frac{p_c}{E}\!\left(\frac{r_m^2 + r_i^2}{r_m^2 - r_i^2} - Î¼\right)
                       + \frac{p_c}{E}\!\left(\frac{r_o^2 + r_m^2}{r_o^2 - r_m^2} - Î¼\right)\right]
  \]

---

## Key Takeaways

- Thick cylinders require **Lameâ€™s variable-stress analysis**; **bore hoop stress** governs.  
- **Compound (shrink-fit) design** smooths stress profile â†’ higher safe pressure.  
- **Closed ends** introduce **longitudinal stress** that must be checked.  
- Consider **thermal effects**, **buckling (external pressure)**, and **fatigue** in real service.  
- Verification with **von Mises** (or code-specified criteria) is best practice.

---


á€á€á€»á€­á€¯á€·á€•á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€¸á€†á€­á€¯á€„á€ºá€›á€¬ (autofrettage/compound) á€€á€­á€¯ á€œá€¯á€•á€ºá€„á€”á€ºá€¸á€á€¯á€¶á€¸á€á€™á€¬á€¸á€¡á€†á€„á€·á€ºá€¡á€‘á€­ á€‘á€Šá€·á€ºá€•á€¼á€á€²á€·á€•á€«á€á€šá€ºá‹

á€¡á€†á€„á€ºá€•á€¼á€±á€•á€«á€á€šá€º âœ…

á€¡á€á€¯á€á€±á€¬á€· Chapter 22 â€“ Rotating Thick Cylinders and Discs (Centrifugal Stresses with Lameâ€™s Theory) á€€á€­á€¯
á€™á€¼á€”á€ºá€™á€¬ + English (technical terms only) á€–á€¼á€„á€·á€º .md format á€”á€²á€· á€•á€¼á€Šá€ºá€·á€…á€¯á€¶á€…á€½á€¬á€›á€±á€¸á€•á€±á€¸á€•á€«á€™á€šá€ºá‹

# âš™ï¸ Chapter 22 â€“ Rotating Thick Cylinders and Discs (Centrifugal Stresses)
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, SI Units)*

---

## **Introduction**

When a **cylinder or disc rotates** at high speed,  
each small element of mass is subjected to **centrifugal force** acting radially outward.  
This causes **tensile stresses** inside the material.

- **Radial stress (Ïƒ_r)** acts perpendicular to the radius.  
- **Hoop (circumferential) stress (Ïƒ_Î¸)** acts tangentially around the rim.  

If rotation speed is too high, these stresses may exceed the materialâ€™s strength,  
causing **bursting or fracture**.  
Hence, the analysis of **rotating discs and cylinders** is critical for **flywheels, turbines, rotors, and centrifuges**.

---

## **Assumptions**

1. Material is **homogeneous** and **isotropic**.  
2. Plane stress condition (thin discs) â€” stress in thickness direction neglected.  
3. Elastic deformation only (within limit of proportionality).  
4. No temperature or external pressure (unless stated).

---

## **Centrifugal Force in a Rotating Element**

For an annular ring of radius \( r \), thickness \( dr \),  
and density \( Ï \), angular velocity \( Ï‰ \):

\[
\text{Centrifugal force on element} = Ï Ï‰^2 r Ã— (2Ï€r t dr)
\]

Where \( t \) = thickness of disc.

This outward force induces **radial stress gradient** inside the material.

---

## **Lame-Type Differential Equation (Equilibrium)**

From radial equilibrium of an element:
\[
\frac{dÏƒ_r}{dr} + \frac{Ïƒ_r - Ïƒ_Î¸}{r} + Ï Ï‰^2 r = 0
\]

This is analogous to Lameâ€™s equation for thick cylinders,  
except that pressure term is replaced by **centrifugal body force**.

---

## **General Solution**

Solving the differential equation gives:
\[
Ïƒ_r = A - \frac{B}{r^2} - \frac{3 + Î¼}{8} Ï Ï‰^2 r^2
\]
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = A + \frac{B}{r^2} - \frac{1 + 3Î¼}{8} Ï Ï‰^2 r^2
\]

where:  
- \( A, B \) = constants from boundary conditions  
- \( Î¼ \) = Poissonâ€™s ratio  
- \( Ï \) = material density  
- \( Ï‰ \) = angular speed (radians/sec)

---

## **Case 1 â€“ Rotating Solid Disc**

Boundary condition:
\[
Ïƒ_r(0) = 0 \quad \text{and} \quad Ïƒ_r(r_o) = 0
\]

From this:
\[
A = \frac{3 + Î¼}{8} Ï Ï‰^2 r_o^2, \quad B = 0
\]

Thus,
\[
Ïƒ_r = \frac{Ï Ï‰^2}{8} [(3 + Î¼)(r_o^2 - r^2)]
\]
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = \frac{Ï Ï‰^2}{8} [(3 + Î¼)r_o^2 - (1 + 3Î¼)r^2]
\]

At center (\(r = 0\)):  
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸(max) = \frac{3 + Î¼}{8} Ï Ï‰^2 r_o^2
\]

So, **maximum hoop stress occurs at the center**.

---

## **Case 2 â€“ Rotating Hollow Disc**

Boundaries:
\[
Ïƒ_r(r_i) = 0, \quad Ïƒ_r(r_o) = 0
\]

Then:
\[
A = \frac{Ï Ï‰^2}{8( r_o^2 - r_i^2)} [(3 + Î¼)(r_o^2 + r_i^2)]
\]
\[
B = \frac{Ï Ï‰^2 r_i^2 r_o^2}{8( r_o^2 - r_i^2)} (3 + Î¼)
\]

Substitute to get:
\[
Ïƒ_r = \frac{Ï Ï‰^2}{8( r_o^2 - r_i^2)}[(3 + Î¼)(r_o^2 + r_i^2 - \frac{r_i^2 r_o^2}{r^2} - r^2)]
\]
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = \frac{Ï Ï‰^2}{8( r_o^2 - r_i^2)}[(3 + Î¼)(r_o^2 + r_i^2 + \frac{r_i^2 r_o^2}{r^2} - (1 + 3Î¼)r^2)]
\]

- \(Ïƒ_Î¸\) maximum â†’ usually at **inner radius (r = r_i)**.  
- \(Ïƒ_r\) is zero at both inner and outer edges.

---

## **Comparison between Solid and Hollow Discs**

| Type | Stress Distribution | Max Stress Location | Strength Ratio |
|------|----------------------|----------------------|----------------|
| Solid disc | Hoop stress highest at center | Center | Lower |
| Hollow disc | Hoop stress highest at inner radius | Inner edge | Higher (lighter for same stress) |

Hence, **hollow rotors** are preferred in high-speed turbines and compressors.

---

## **Bursting Speed (Safe Operating Limit)**

At the limiting (burst) speed,  
maximum hoop stress = allowable stress \(Ïƒ_{allow}\):

\[
Ïƒ_{Î¸(max)} = \frac{3 + Î¼}{8} Ï Ï‰^2 r_o^2 = Ïƒ_{allow}
\]

Solve for \( Ï‰ \):
\[
Ï‰ = \sqrt{\frac{8 Ïƒ_{allow}}{Ï (3 + Î¼) r_o^2}}
\]

Rotational speed in revolutions per second:
\[
N = \frac{Ï‰}{2Ï€}
\]

> Used to determine **safe rotational speed** of discs and flywheels.

---

## **Flywheel Rim Analysis**

For flywheels, rim mass provides energy storage but creates hoop tension.

- Neglecting spokes and arms:
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = Ï Ï‰^2 r^2
\]

Including arms (which restrain expansion slightly):
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ = k Ï Ï‰^2 r^2, \quad \text{where } k â‰ˆ 0.75
\]

**Safe design rule**  
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ \le Ïƒ_{allow} / FOS
\]
FOS = 3 for cast iron, 5 for steel rims.

---

## **Effect of Poissonâ€™s Ratio (Î¼)**

The difference between hoop and radial stresses:
\[
Ïƒ_Î¸ - Ïƒ_r = \frac{Ï Ï‰^2 r^2}{4}(1 + Î¼)
\]
Hence, increasing \( Î¼ \) slightly increases the hoop stress magnitude.  
Typical \( Î¼ = 0.25 â€“ 0.3 \).

---

## **Example Calculation**

**Data:**  
- Steel disc, \( r_o = 200\,mm \), solid  
- \( Ï = 7800\,kg/m^3 \), \( Î¼ = 0.3 \), \( Ïƒ_{allow} = 150\,MPa \)

Find safe \( Ï‰ \):

\[
Ï‰ = \sqrt{\frac{8 Ã— 150Ã—10^6}{7800 Ã— (3.3) Ã— 0.2^2}} = 972\,rad/s
\]
\[
N = \frac{972}{2Ï€} = 155\,rev/s = 9300\,rpm
\]

Hence safe speed â‰ˆ **9300 rpm**.

---

## **Practical Design Notes**

- Always check **maximum hoop stress** at bore (hollow discs).  
- Provide **fillet radius** at inner bore to avoid stress concentration.  
- **Shrink-fit compound rotors** can reduce hoop stress peak.  
- Balance discs dynamically to prevent vibration and fatigue.  
- Apply **thermal stress correction** in hot-running turbines.

---

## **Key Formula Summary**

| Parameter | Solid Disc | Hollow Disc | Units |
|------------|-------------|-------------|--------|
| \(Ïƒ_r\) | \( \frac{Ï Ï‰^2}{8}[(3+Î¼)(r_o^2 - r^2)] \) | see general formula | Pa |
| \(Ïƒ_Î¸\) | \( \frac{Ï Ï‰^2}{8}[(3+Î¼)r_o^2 - (1+3Î¼)r^2] \) | see general formula | Pa |
| \(Ïƒ_{Î¸(max)}\) | \( \frac{3+Î¼}{8}Ï Ï‰^2 r_o^2 \) | at \(r_i\) | Pa |
| Safe speed | \( Ï‰ = \sqrt{\frac{8 Ïƒ_{allow}}{Ï (3+Î¼) r_o^2}} \) |  | rad/s |

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- **Centrifugal stresses** arise due to rotation; they act **tensile** in nature.  
- **Hoop stress > Radial stress** â†’ governs design.  
- For same material and speed, **hollow discs** are stronger and lighter.  
- Always verify **burst speed** against allowable tensile stress.  
- Used widely in **flywheels, turbine rotors, and centrifuges**.  

---

> â€œRotation tests a materialâ€™s courage â€” tension fights outward, but design holds it together.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*

# âš™ï¸ Chapter 23 â€“ Torsional Vibrations and Shaft Dynamics  
*(From â€œStrength of Materialsâ€ by G.H. Ryder, SI Units)*  

---

## **Introduction**

In rotating machinery (motors, turbines, generators, propeller shafts, etc.),  
the **shaft** is often subjected to **torsional vibrations** caused by cyclic torque or uneven driving forces.  
These vibrations can lead to **fatigue failure**, **noise**, and **loss of efficiency**.  

To ensure safe operation, itâ€™s essential to analyze:
- **Natural frequency** of the shaft,
- **Critical speed** (resonance condition), and  
- **Amplitude of torsional oscillation**.

---

## **Basic Concept**

When a shaft is twisted by an applied torque \( T \),  
it stores **strain energy** due to its **torsional stiffness** \( k_t \).  

If the torque varies periodically, the shaft behaves like a **torsional spring** with an attached **rotating mass** (flywheel).  
This produces **angular vibration** about the mean position â€” called *torsional vibration*.  

---

## **Torsional Stiffness (kâ‚œ)**

\[
k_t = \frac{GJ}{l}
\]

where:
- \( G \) = Modulus of rigidity (Shear modulus)  
- \( J \) = Polar moment of inertia of shaft cross-section  
- \( l \) = Length of shaft  

For a solid circular shaft:
\[
J = \frac{Ï€ d^4}{32}
\]

---

## **Single-Rotor System (Single Mass Torsional Vibration)**

Consider a shaft with one **rotor of moment of inertia** \( I \) attached to its end.  
If it twists through an angle \( Î¸ \), the **restoring torque** is:

\[
T = k_t Î¸ = \frac{GJ}{l} Î¸
\]

Equation of motion:
\[
I \frac{d^2Î¸}{dt^2} + k_t Î¸ = 0
\]

This is a simple harmonic motion (SHM) with **natural angular frequency**:

\[
Ï‰_n = \sqrt{\frac{k_t}{I}} = \sqrt{\frac{GJ}{I l}}
\]

and **natural period**:
\[
T_n = 2Ï€ \sqrt{\frac{I l}{GJ}}
\]

---

## **Two-Rotor System**

If two rotors \( I_1 \) and \( I_2 \) are attached at the ends of a shaft of length \( l \):  

Let \( Î¸_1, Î¸_2 \) be angular displacements at each end.  
Then torque transmitted through the shaft is:

\[
T = \frac{GJ}{l} (Î¸_1 - Î¸_2)
\]

Equations of motion:
\[
I_1 \frac{d^2Î¸_1}{dt^2} = -T, \quad I_2 \frac{d^2Î¸_2}{dt^2} = T
\]

For **free vibration**, combining and simplifying:
\[
Ï‰_n = \sqrt{ \frac{GJ}{l} \left( \frac{1}{I_1} + \frac{1}{I_2} \right) }
\]

and the **node position** (no angular movement point) divides the shaft in inverse ratio of the moments of inertia:

\[
\frac{l_1}{l_2} = \sqrt{\frac{I_1}{I_2}}
\]

---

## **Torsional Resonance (Critical Speed)**

When the **forcing frequency (Ï‰)** equals **natural frequency (Ï‰â‚™)**,  
the amplitude of vibration increases sharply â€” this condition is called **torsional resonance**.

To prevent resonance:
- Design shaft with **Ï‰â‚™ > operating speed**, or  
- Add damping (by couplings or torsional dampers).

---

## **Energy Method**

Energy stored in a twisted shaft:
\[
U = \frac{1}{2} T Î¸ = \frac{1}{2} \frac{GJ}{l} Î¸^2
\]

At the extreme displacement, this potential energy equals the kinetic energy of the rotor:
\[
\frac{1}{2} I Ï‰_n^2 Î¸^2 = \frac{1}{2} \frac{GJ}{l} Î¸^2
\]

Hence:
\[
Ï‰_n = \sqrt{\frac{GJ}{I l}}
\]

This confirms the earlier result from differential analysis.

---

## **Critical Speed of Shaft**

In long rotating shafts (e.g., turbine or propeller shafts),  
torsional vibration can couple with **bending vibrations**, producing **critical speed** conditions.

Critical speed (for shaft without damping):
\[
N_c = \frac{Ï‰_n}{2Ï€} = \frac{1}{2Ï€} \sqrt{\frac{GJ}{I l}}
\]

If operating speed â‰ˆ critical speed â†’ large torsional oscillation â†’ failure possible.

---

## **Multi-Rotor Systems**

For more than two rotors, equations become:
\[
I_i \frac{d^2Î¸_i}{dt^2} + k_i(Î¸_i - Î¸_{i-1}) + k_{i+1}(Î¸_i - Î¸_{i+1}) = 0
\]

Can be solved by:
- **Matrix method**, or  
- **Holzerâ€™s method** (tabular iterative approach).  

The lowest natural frequency gives the **fundamental mode**,  
and higher frequencies represent **harmonic modes**.

---

## **Damping in Torsional Systems**

Although most torsional systems are lightly damped,  
some energy is dissipated due to:
- Internal friction,  
- Oil couplings,  
- Rubber or viscous dampers.

**Damped vibration equation:**
\[
I \frac{d^2Î¸}{dt^2} + c \frac{dÎ¸}{dt} + k_t Î¸ = 0
\]

Where \( c \) = damping coefficient.

---

## **Flywheel Effect**

A **flywheel** connected to the shaft helps smoothen torque variations.  
However, it increases the systemâ€™s **moment of inertia (I)** and can **lower natural frequency**.  
Hence, design must balance between energy storage and torsional safety.

---

## **Practical Design Considerations**

- Locate **nodes** near gearboxes or couplings to minimize transmitted vibration.  
- Use **rubber couplings** or **torsional dampers** to absorb shock.  
- Avoid operating near **critical speed zones**.  
- Employ **finite element analysis (FEA)** for modern shaft design.

---

## **Key Formula Summary**

| Parameter | Formula | Description |
|------------|----------|-------------|
| Torsional stiffness | \( k_t = \frac{GJ}{l} \) | Shaftâ€™s resistance to twist |
| Natural frequency | \( Ï‰_n = \sqrt{\frac{GJ}{I l}} \) | For single rotor |
| Node ratio | \( \frac{l_1}{l_2} = \sqrt{\frac{I_1}{I_2}} \) | Two-rotor system |
| Critical speed | \( N_c = \frac{1}{2Ï€}\sqrt{\frac{GJ}{I l}} \) | Shaft vibration resonance |
| Energy stored | \( U = \frac{1}{2} \frac{GJ}{l} Î¸^2 \) | Elastic strain energy |

---

## **Key Takeaways**

- **Torsional vibrations** arise due to fluctuating torque in rotating shafts.  
- Resonance must be avoided to prevent **shaft failure**.  
- Natural frequency depends on **shaft stiffness (GJ/l)** and **rotor inertia (I)**.  
- For safety, always ensure \( Ï‰_{operating} < 0.8 Ï‰_n \).  
- Use **damping and flexible couplings** for smoother operation.  

---

> â€œEvery rotating machine has a rhythm â€”  
> control its vibration, and you control its life.â€  
> â€” *G.H. Ryder*
